The definition is similar to that of post-quantum extractable commitments in [\[BS20,](#page-58-4) [BLS21\]](#page-58-6) except that we allow an (arbitrarily small) noticeable approximation error similarly to post-quantum ε-zero-knowledge [\[CCY21\]](#page-58-7). We note that we call it the strong extractability since we also define a weaker version of extractability in [Def. 13](#page-30-0) in [Sec. 5.1.](#page-29-1)

<span id="page-20-2"></span>Definition 11 (Strong Extractability with ε-Simulation). A commitment scheme Π is strongly extractable with ε-simulation if there exists a QPT algorithm SE (called the ε-simulation strongextractor) such that for any noticeable ε(λ) and any non-uniform QPT C ∗ (ρ),

<span id="page-20-3"></span>
$$\left\{\mathcal{SE}^{C^*(\rho)}(1^{\lambda},1^{\varepsilon^{-1}})\right\}_{\lambda} \overset{c}{\approx}_{\varepsilon} \left\{ (\mathsf{val}_{\Pi}(\mathsf{com}),\mathsf{ST}_{C^*}) : (\mathsf{com},\mathsf{ST}_{C^*},b_{\mathsf{com}}) \leftarrow \langle C^*(\rho),R\rangle(1^{\lambda}) \right\}_{\lambda}.$$

We also define the parallel version.

Definition 12 (Parallel-Strong Extractability with ε-Simulation). A commitment scheme Π is parallelly strongly extractable with ε-simulation if for any integer n = poly(λ), there exists a QPT algorithm SEpar (called the ε-simulation parallel-strong-extractor) such that for any noticeable ε(λ) and any non-uniform QPT C ∗ (ρ),

$$\begin{split} & \left\{ \mathcal{SE}_{\mathsf{par}}^{C^*(\rho)}(1^{\lambda},1^{\varepsilon^{-1}}) \right\}_{\lambda} \\ & \stackrel{c}{\approx}_{\varepsilon} \left\{ (\varLambda_{\{b_{\mathsf{com},j}\}_{j=1}^n}(\{\mathsf{val}(\mathsf{com}_j)\}_{j=1}^n),\mathsf{ST}_{C^*}) : (\{\mathsf{com}_j\}_{j=1}^n,\mathsf{ST}_{C^*},\{b_{\mathsf{com},j}\}_{j=1}^n) \leftarrow \langle C^*(\rho),R^n\rangle(1^{\lambda}) \right\}_{\lambda} \end{split}$$

{21}------------------------------------------------

where  $(\{\mathsf{com}_j\}_{j=1}^n, \mathsf{ST}_{C^*}, \{b_{\mathsf{com},j}\}_{j=1}^n) \leftarrow \langle C^*(\rho), R^n \rangle (1^{\lambda})$  means that  $C^*(\rho)$  interacts with n copies of the honest receiver R in parallel and the execution results in transcripts  $\{\mathsf{com}_j\}_{j=1}^n$ , the final state  $\mathsf{ST}_{C^*}$ , and outputs  $\{b_{\mathsf{com},j}\}_{j=1}^n$  of each copy of R and

$$\varLambda_{\{b_{\mathrm{com},j}\}_{j=1}^n}(\{\mathrm{val}(\mathsf{com}_j)\}_{j=1}^n) \coloneqq \begin{cases} \{\mathrm{val}_\Pi(\mathsf{com}_j)\}_{j=1}^n & \textit{if} \ \forall \ j \in [n] \ b_{\mathrm{com},j} = 1 \\ \bot & \textit{otherwise} \end{cases}.$$

Remark 4. We remark that the above definition only requires the extractor to extract the committed values when R accepts in all the parallel sessions. In particular, when R accepts in some sessions but not in others, the extractor does not need to extract the committed values at all. An alternative stronger (and probably more natural) definition would require the extractor to extract  $\mathsf{val}_\Pi(\mathsf{com}_j)$  for all  $j \in [n]$  such that R accepts in the j-th session. But we define it in the above way since it suffices for our purpose and we do not know if our construction satisfies the stronger one.

## <span id="page-21-0"></span>4 Extract-and-Simulate Lemma

We prove a lemma that can be seen as an  $\varepsilon$ -simulation variant of Unruh's rewinding lemma (Lem. 3) in typical applications. This lemma is the technical core of all the results in this paper.

## <span id="page-21-1"></span>4.1 Statement of Extract-and-Simulate Lemma

Our lemma is stated as follows.

**Lemma 4 (Extract-and-Simulate Lemma).** Let C be a finite set. Let  $\{\Pi_i\}_{i\in C}$  be orthogonal projectors on a Hilbert space  $\mathcal{H}$  such that the measurement  $\{\Pi_i, I - \Pi_i\}$  can be efficiently implemented. Let  $|\psi_{\mathsf{init}}\rangle \in \mathcal{H}$  be a unit vector.

Suppose that there are a subset  $S \in C^2$  and a QPT algorithm  $\mathcal{A} = (\mathcal{A}_0, \mathcal{A}_1)$  that satisfies the following:

- 1. S consists of an overwhelming fraction of  $C^2$ , i.e.,  $\frac{|S|}{|C|^2} = 1 \text{negl}(\lambda)$ .
- <span id="page-21-4"></span>2. For all  $i \in C$ , there exists a classical string  $s_i$  such that

<span id="page-21-2"></span>
$$\Pr\left[\mathcal{A}_0\left(i, \frac{\Pi_i |\psi_{\mathsf{init}}\rangle}{\|\Pi_i |\psi_{\mathsf{init}}\rangle\|}\right) = s_i\right] = 1.$$

<span id="page-21-5"></span>3. There exists a classical string  $s^*$  such that for any  $(i, j) \in S$ ,

$$\Pr\left[\mathcal{A}_1\left(i,j,s_i,s_j\right) = s^*\right] = 1.$$

Let  $\mathsf{Exp}(\lambda, \{\Pi_i\}_{i \in C}, |\psi_{\mathsf{init}}\rangle)$  be an experiment that works as follows:

- Choose  $i \leftarrow C$ .
- Apply the measurement  $\{\Pi_i, I \Pi_i\}$  on  $|\psi_{\mathsf{init}}\rangle$ .
  - If the state is projected onto  $\Pi_i$ , the experiment outputs i, the classical string  $s^*$ , and the resulting state  $\frac{\Pi_i |\psi_{\mathsf{init}}\rangle}{\|\Pi_i |\psi_{\mathsf{init}}\rangle\|}$ .
  - If the state is projected onto  $I-\Pi_i$ , the experiment outputs  $i, \perp$ , and the resulting state  $\frac{(I-\Pi_i)|\psi_{\mathsf{init}}\rangle}{|(I-\Pi_i)|\psi_{\mathsf{init}}\rangle|}$ . Then, there is a QPT algorithm  $\mathcal{SE}$  such that for any noticeable  $\varepsilon$ ,

$$\{\mathcal{SE}(1^{\lambda}, 1^{\varepsilon^{-1}}, \{\Pi_i\}_{i \in C}, \mathcal{A}, |\psi_{\mathsf{init}}\rangle)\}_{\lambda} \stackrel{s}{\approx}_{\varepsilon} \{\mathsf{Exp}(\lambda, \{\Pi_i\}_{i \in C}, |\psi_{\mathsf{init}}\rangle)\}_{\lambda}.$$

<span id="page-21-3"></span>We stress that we do not assume that the experiment is efficient. Especially, it may be computationally hard to find  $s^*$  from  $\frac{\Pi_i |\psi_{\text{init}}\rangle}{\|\Pi_i |\psi_{\text{init}}\rangle\|}$ .

{22}------------------------------------------------

### <span id="page-22-4"></span><span id="page-22-0"></span>4.2 Proof of the Extract-and-Simulate Lemma

We prove the extract-and-simulate lemma (Lem. 4). Throughout this subsection, we use the notations defined in Lem. 4.

Structure of the Proof. The high-level structure of our proof is similar to those of the  $(\varepsilon$ -)zero-knowledge properties of protocols in [BS20, CCY21]. We first construct extractors  $\operatorname{Ext}_{\operatorname{Sim,a}}$  and  $\operatorname{Ext}_{\operatorname{Sim,na}}$  that work in the "aborting case" and "non-aborting case", respectively where we say that the experiment  $\operatorname{Exp}(\lambda, \{\Pi_i\}_{i \in C}, |\psi_{\operatorname{init}}\rangle)$  aborts if its second output is  $\bot$ . Then we consider a combined simulator  $\operatorname{Ext}_{\operatorname{Sim,comb}}$  that randomly guesses if the experiment aborts, runs either of  $\operatorname{Ext}_{\operatorname{Sim,a}}$  or  $\operatorname{Ext}_{\operatorname{Sim,na}}$  that corresponds to the guessed case, and returns a failure symbol Fail if the guess turns out to be wrong. Then,  $\operatorname{Ext}_{\operatorname{comb}}$  correctly works conditioned on that the output is not Fail, and it returns Fail with probability almost 1/2. By applying Watrous' rewinding lemma ( Lem. 2) to  $\operatorname{Ext}_{\operatorname{Sim,comb}}$ , we can convert it to a full-fledged simulator.

Let  $\mathsf{Exp}_{\mathsf{a}}(\lambda,\{\Pi_i\}_{i\in C},|\psi_{\mathsf{init}}\rangle)$  and  $\mathsf{Exp}_{\mathsf{na}}(\lambda,\{\Pi_i\}_{i\in C},|\psi_{\mathsf{init}}\rangle)$  be the same as  $\mathsf{Exp}(\lambda,\{\Pi_i\}_{i\in C},|\psi_{\mathsf{init}}\rangle)$  except that they output a failure symbol Fail in aborting and non-aborting case, respectively. That is, they work as follows where differences from  $\mathsf{Exp}(\lambda,\{\Pi_i\}_{i\in C},|\psi_{\mathsf{init}}\rangle)$  are marked by red underlines:  $\mathsf{Exp}_{\mathsf{a}}(\lambda,\{\Pi_i\}_{i\in C},|\psi_{\mathsf{init}}\rangle)$ :

- Choose  $i \leftarrow C$ .
- Apply the measurement  $\{\Pi_i, I \Pi_i\}$  on  $|\psi_{\mathsf{init}}\rangle$ .
  - If the state is projected onto  $\Pi_i$ , the experiment outputs Fail.
  - If the state is projected onto  $I \Pi_i$ , the experiment outputs i,  $\bot$ , and the resulting state  $\frac{(I-\Pi_i)|\psi_{\mathsf{init}}\rangle}{\|(I-\Pi_i)|\psi_{\mathsf{init}}\rangle\|}$ .

 $\mathsf{Exp}_{\mathsf{na}}(\lambda, \{\Pi_i\}_{i \in C}, |\psi_{\mathsf{init}}\rangle)$ :

- Choose  $i \leftarrow C$ .
- Apply the measurement  $\{\Pi_i, I \Pi_i\}$  on  $|\psi_{\mathsf{init}}\rangle$ .
  - If the state is projected onto  $\Pi_i$ , the experiment outputs i, the classical string  $s^*$ , and the resulting state  $\frac{\Pi_i |\psi_{\mathsf{init}}\rangle}{\|\Pi_i |\psi_{\mathsf{init}}\rangle\|}$ .
  - If the state is projected onto  $I \Pi_i$ , the experiment outputs Fail.

We give simulation extractors for each of these experiments.

Lemma 5 (Extract-and-Simulate for the Aborting Case). There is a QPT algorithm  $Ext_{Sim,a}$  such that for any noticeable  $\varepsilon$ ,

<span id="page-22-3"></span>
$$\{\mathsf{Ext}_{\mathsf{Sim},\mathsf{a}}(1^{\lambda},1^{\varepsilon^{-1}},\{\Pi_i\}_{i\in C},\mathcal{A},|\psi_{\mathsf{init}}\rangle)\}_{\lambda} \equiv \{\mathsf{Exp}_{\mathsf{a}}(\lambda,\{\Pi_i\}_{i\in C},|\psi_{\mathsf{init}}\rangle)\}_{\lambda}.^{23}$$

*Proof of Lem. 5.* Since  $\mathsf{Exp}_\mathsf{a}$  can be run efficiently (because it never outputs  $s^*$ ),  $\mathsf{Ext}_\mathsf{Sim,a}$  just needs to run  $\mathsf{Exp}_\mathsf{a}$ .

<span id="page-22-1"></span>Lemma 6 (Extract-and-Simulate for the Non-aborting Case). There is a QPT algorithm  $\operatorname{Ext}_{\mathsf{Sim},\mathsf{na}}$  such that for any noticeable  $\varepsilon$ ,

$$\{\mathsf{Ext}_{\mathsf{Sim},\mathsf{na}}(1^{\lambda},1^{\varepsilon^{-1}},\{\Pi_i\}_{i\in C},\mathcal{A},|\psi_{\mathsf{init}}\rangle)\}_{\lambda} \overset{s}{\approx}_{\varepsilon} \{\mathsf{Exp}_{\mathsf{na}}(\lambda,\{\Pi_i\}_{i\in C},|\psi_{\mathsf{init}}\rangle)\}_{\lambda}.$$

<span id="page-22-2"></span><sup>&</sup>lt;sup>23</sup>  $\mathsf{Ext}_{\mathsf{Sim,a}}$  does not need to take  $1^{\varepsilon^{-1}}$  or  $\mathcal{A}$  as part of its input, but we include them in input for notational convenience.

{23}------------------------------------------------

<span id="page-23-3"></span>Since the proof of Lem. 6 is the most non-trivial technical part, we defer it to Sec. 4.4 after some preparations in Sec. 4.3.

Given Lem. 5 and Lem. 6, the rest of the proof of Lem. 4 is very similar to the corresponding part of the  $\varepsilon$ -zero-knowledge property of the protocols in [CCY21]. We give the full proof for completeness.

Let Ext<sub>Sim,comb</sub> be an algorithm that works as follows:

 $\mathsf{Ext}_{\mathsf{Sim},\mathsf{comb}}(1^{\lambda},1^{\varepsilon^{-1}},\{\Pi_i\}_{i\in C},\mathcal{A},|\psi_{\mathsf{init}}\rangle)$ :

- 1. Set  $\varepsilon' := \frac{\varepsilon^2}{3600 \log^4(\lambda)}$ .
- 2. Choose mode  $\leftarrow \{a, na\}$ .
- 3. Run and output  $\mathsf{Ext}_{\mathsf{Sim},\mathsf{mode}}(1^{\lambda},1^{\varepsilon'^{-1}},\{\Pi_i\}_{i\in C},\mathcal{A},|\psi_{\mathsf{init}}\rangle)$ .

<span id="page-23-1"></span>Lemma 7 (Ext<sub>Sim,comb</sub> Simulates Exp with Probability almost 1/2). Let  $p_{\mathsf{comb}}^{\mathsf{suc}}(1^{\lambda}, 1^{\varepsilon^{-1}}, \{\Pi_i\}_{i \in C}, \mathcal{A}, |\psi_{\mathsf{init}}\rangle)$  be the probability that  $\mathsf{Ext}_{\mathsf{Sim,comb}}(1^{\lambda}, 1^{\varepsilon^{-1}}, \{\Pi_i\}_{i \in C}, \mathcal{A}, |\psi_{\mathsf{init}}\rangle)$  does not return Fail, and let

<span id="page-23-2"></span><span id="page-23-0"></span>
$$D_{\mathsf{ext.comb}}(1^{\lambda}, 1^{\varepsilon^{-1}}, \{\Pi_i\}_{i \in C}, \mathcal{A}, |\psi_{\mathsf{init}}\rangle)$$

be a conditional distribution of  $\mathsf{Ext}_{\mathsf{Sim},\mathsf{comb}}(1^{\lambda},1^{\varepsilon^{-1}},\{\Pi_i\}_{i\in C},\mathcal{A},|\psi_{\mathsf{init}}\rangle)$ , conditioned on that it does not return Fail. Then we have

$$\left| p_{\mathsf{comb}}^{\mathsf{suc}}(1^{\lambda}, 1^{\varepsilon^{-1}}, \{ \Pi_i \}_{i \in C}, \mathcal{A}, |\psi_{\mathsf{init}}\rangle) - 1/2 \right| \le \varepsilon'/2 + \mathsf{negl}(\lambda). \tag{4}$$

Moreover, we have

$$\{D_{\mathsf{ext},\mathsf{comb}}(1^{\lambda},1^{\varepsilon^{-1}},\{\Pi_i\}_{i\in C},\mathcal{A},|\psi_{\mathsf{init}}\rangle)\}_{\lambda} \stackrel{s}{\approx}_{4\varepsilon'} \{\mathsf{Exp}(\lambda,\{\Pi_i\}_{i\in C},|\psi_{\mathsf{init}}\rangle)\}_{\lambda}. \tag{5}$$

*Proof.* (sketch.) The intuition behind this proof is as follows. By Lemma 5 and 6, Ext<sub>Sim,a</sub> and Ext<sub>Sim,na</sub> almost simulate Exp conditioned on that Exp aborts and does not abort, respectively. Therefore, if we randomly guess if Exp aborts and runs either of Ext<sub>Sim,a</sub> or Ext<sub>Sim,na</sub> that successfully works for the guessed case, the output distribution is close to the real output distribution of Exp conditioned on that the guess is correct, which happens with probability almost 1/2.

A formal proof can be obtained based on the above intuition and is exactly the same as the proof of [CCY21, Lemma 5.5] except for notational adaptations.  $\Box$ 

Then, we convert  $\operatorname{Ext}_{\operatorname{Sim},\operatorname{comb}}$  into a full-fledged simulator that does not return Fail by using Watrous' rewinding lemma (Lemma 2). Namely, we let Q be a quantum algorithm that takes  $|\psi_{\operatorname{init}}\rangle$  as input and outputs  $\operatorname{Ext}_{\operatorname{Sim},\operatorname{comb}}(1^{\lambda},1^{\varepsilon^{-1}},\{\Pi_{i}\}_{i\in C},\mathcal{A},|\psi_{\operatorname{init}}\rangle)$  where b:=0 if and only if it does not return Fail,  $p_{0}:=\frac{1}{4},\,q:=\frac{1}{2},\,\gamma:=\varepsilon',\,$  and  $T:=2\log(1/\varepsilon').$  Then it is easy to check that the conditions for Lemma 2 is satisfied by Eq. 4 in Lemma 7 (for sufficiently large  $\lambda$ ). Then by using Lemma 2, we can see that  $\operatorname{R}(1^{T},\operatorname{Q},|\psi_{\operatorname{init}}\rangle)$  runs in time  $T\cdot|\operatorname{Q}|=\operatorname{poly}(\lambda)$  and its output (seen as a mixed state) has a trace distance bounded by  $4\sqrt{\gamma}\frac{\log(1/\gamma)}{p_{0}(1-p_{0})}$  from  $D_{\operatorname{ext},\operatorname{comb}}(1^{\lambda},1^{\varepsilon^{-1}},\{\Pi_{i}\}_{i\in C},\mathcal{A},|\psi_{\operatorname{init}}\rangle)$ . Since we have  $\gamma=\varepsilon'=\frac{\varepsilon^{2}}{3600\log^{4}(\lambda)}=1/\operatorname{poly}(\lambda)$ , we have  $4\sqrt{\gamma}\frac{\log(1/\gamma)}{p_{0}(1-p_{0})}<30\sqrt{\gamma}\log^{2}(\lambda)=\frac{\varepsilon}{2}$  for sufficiently large  $\lambda$  where we used  $\log(1/\gamma)=\log(\operatorname{poly}(\lambda))=o(\log^{2}(\lambda))$ . Thus, by combining the above and Eq. 5 in Lemma 7, if we define  $\operatorname{Ext}_{\operatorname{Sim}}(1^{\lambda},1^{\varepsilon^{-1}},\{\Pi_{i}\}_{i\in C},\mathcal{A},|\psi_{\operatorname{init}}\rangle):=\operatorname{R}(1^{T},\operatorname{Q},|\psi_{\operatorname{init}}\rangle)$ , then we have

$$\{\mathsf{Ext}_{\mathsf{Sim}}(1^{\lambda},1^{\varepsilon^{-1}},\{\Pi_i\}_{i\in C},\mathcal{A},|\psi_{\mathsf{init}}\rangle)\}_{\lambda} \overset{\mathrm{s}}{\approx} \frac{\varepsilon}{2} + 4\varepsilon' \ \{\mathsf{Exp}(\lambda,\{\Pi_i\}_{i\in C},|\psi_{\mathsf{init}}\rangle)\}_{\lambda}.$$

We can conclude the proof of Lem. 4 by noting that we have  $\frac{\varepsilon}{2} + 4\varepsilon' < \varepsilon$  since we have  $\varepsilon' = \frac{\varepsilon^2}{3600 \log^4(\lambda)} < \frac{\varepsilon}{8}$ .

{24}------------------------------------------------

### <span id="page-24-7"></span><span id="page-24-0"></span>4.3 Preparation for Proof of Lem. 6

For proving Lem. 6, we prepare the following three lemmas.

The first is a simple technical lemma that is a variant of [CCY21, Lemma 3.1].

**Lemma 8 (Variant of [CCY21, Lemma 3.1]).** Let  $|\phi_b\rangle = |\phi_{b,0}\rangle + |\phi_{b,1}\rangle$  be a normalized quantum state in a Hilbert space  $\mathcal{H}$ . Let F be a quantum algorithm that takes a state in  $\mathcal{H}$  as input and outputs a quantum state (not necessarily in  $\mathcal{H}$ ) or a classical failure symbol Fail. Suppose that we have

<span id="page-24-2"></span>
$$\Pr \left[ F \left( \frac{\left| \phi_{b,0} \right\rangle \left\langle \phi_{b,0} \right|}{\| \left| \phi_{b,0} \right\rangle \|^2} \right) = \mathsf{Fail} \right] \geq 1 - \gamma$$

for  $b \in \{0,1\}$  and  $\||\phi_{1,1}\rangle - |\phi_{1,0}\rangle\| \le \delta$ . Then for any distinguisher D, it holds that  $d^{24}$ 

$$|\Pr[D(F(|\phi_0\rangle \langle \phi_0|)) = 1] - \Pr[D(F(|\phi_1\rangle \langle \phi_1|)) = 1]| \le (12\gamma^{1/2} + 2\delta)^{1/2}.$$

We give the proof in Appx. B.2.

The second lemma is a variant of the gentle measurement lemma shown in [CCY21].

Lemma 9 ([CCY21, Lemma 3.2]). Let  $|\psi\rangle_{\mathbf{X}}$  be a (not necessarily normalized) state over register  $\mathbf{X}$  and U be a unitary over registers  $(\mathbf{X}, \mathbf{Y}, \mathbf{Z})$ . Suppose that a measurement of register  $\mathbf{Z}$  of  $U |\psi\rangle_{\mathbf{X}} |0\rangle_{\mathbf{Y},\mathbf{Z}}$  results in a deterministic value except for probability  $\nu$ , i.e., there is  $z^*$  such that

<span id="page-24-6"></span>
$$\|(I - |z^*\rangle \langle z^*|)_{\mathbf{Z}} U |\psi\rangle_{\mathbf{X}} |0\rangle_{\mathbf{Y},\mathbf{Z}}\|^2 \le \nu.$$

If we let  $R := (|0\rangle \langle 0|)_{\mathbf{Y},\mathbf{Z}} U^{\dagger}(|z^*\rangle \langle z^*|)_{\mathbf{Z}} U$ , then we have

<span id="page-24-1"></span>
$$\| |\psi\rangle_{\mathbf{X}} |0\rangle_{\mathbf{Y},\mathbf{Z}} - R |\psi\rangle_{\mathbf{X}} |0\rangle_{\mathbf{Y},\mathbf{Z}} \| \leq \sqrt{\nu}.$$

The third is a variant of [CCY21, Lemma 3.3].

Lemma 10 (A variant of [CCY21, Lemma 3.3]). Let  $\Pi$  be a projection over a Hilbert space  $\mathcal{H}_{\mathbf{X}} \otimes \mathcal{H}_{\mathbf{Y}}$ . For any noticeable function  $\delta = \delta(\lambda)$ , there exists an orthogonal decomposition  $(S_{<\delta}, S_{\geq \delta})$  of  $\mathcal{H}_{\mathbf{X}} \otimes \mathcal{H}_{\mathbf{Y}}$  that satisfies the following:

<span id="page-24-5"></span>1.  $(S_{<\delta} \text{ and } S_{\geq \delta} \text{ are invariant under } \Pi \text{ and } (|0\rangle\langle 0|)_{\mathbf{Y}}.)$  For any  $|\psi\rangle_{\mathbf{X},\mathbf{Y}} \in S_{<\delta}$ , we have

$$\Pi |\psi\rangle_{\mathbf{X},\mathbf{Y}} \in S_{<\delta}, \qquad (I_{\mathbf{X}} \otimes (|0\rangle\langle 0|)_{\mathbf{Y}}) |\psi\rangle_{\mathbf{X},\mathbf{Y}} \in S_{<\delta}.$$

Similarly, for any  $|\psi\rangle_{\mathbf{X},\mathbf{Y}} \in S_{\geq \delta}$ , we have

$$\Pi |\psi\rangle_{\mathbf{X},\mathbf{Y}} \in S_{\geq \delta}, \qquad (I_{\mathbf{X}} \otimes (|0\rangle \langle 0|)_{\mathbf{Y}}) |\psi\rangle_{\mathbf{X},\mathbf{Y}} \in S_{\geq \delta}.$$

<span id="page-24-4"></span>2. ( $\Pi$  succeeds with probability  $<\delta$  and  $\geq \delta$  in  $S_{<\delta}$  and  $S_{\geq \delta}$ .) For any quantum state  $|\phi\rangle_{\mathbf{X}} \in \mathcal{H}_{\mathbf{X}}$  s.t.  $|\phi\rangle_{\mathbf{X}} |0\rangle_{\mathbf{Y}} \in S_{<\delta}$  we have

$$\|\Pi |\phi\rangle_{\mathbf{X}} |0\rangle_{\mathbf{Y}}\|^2 < \delta.$$

Similarly, for any quantum state  $|\phi\rangle_{\mathbf{X}} \in \mathcal{H}_{\mathbf{X}}$  s.t.  $|\phi\rangle_{\mathbf{X}} |0\rangle_{\mathbf{Y}} \in S_{\geq \delta}$  we have

$$\|\Pi |\phi\rangle_{\mathbf{X}} |0\rangle_{\mathbf{Y}}\|^2 \ge \delta.$$

<span id="page-24-3"></span>In the previous version of this paper, we claimed that the bound was  $4\gamma^{1/4} + \| |\phi_{0,1}\rangle \langle \phi_{0,1}| - |\phi_{1,1}\rangle \langle \phi_{1,1}| \|_{tr}$ , but there was a flaw in the proof in the case of  $|\phi_{0,1}\rangle \neq |\phi_{1,1}\rangle$ .

{25}------------------------------------------------

- <span id="page-25-7"></span><span id="page-25-6"></span><span id="page-25-1"></span>3. (Unitary for amplification.) For any  $T \in \mathbb{N}$ , there exists a unitary  $U_{\mathsf{amp},T}$  over  $\mathcal{H}_{\mathbf{X}} \otimes \mathcal{H}_{\mathbf{Y}} \otimes \mathcal{H}_{\mathbf{B}} \otimes \mathcal{H}_{\mathbf{Anc}}$  where  $\mathbf{B}$  is a register to store a qubit and  $\mathbf{Anc}$  is a register to store ancillary qubits with the following properties:
  - (a) (Mapped onto  $\Pi(I_{\mathbf{X}} \otimes (|0\rangle \langle 0|)_{\mathbf{Y}})$  when B contains 1.) For any quantum state  $|\psi\rangle_{\mathbf{X},\mathbf{Y}} \in \mathcal{H}_{\mathbf{X}} \otimes \mathcal{H}_{\mathbf{Y}}$ , we can write

$$|1\rangle \langle 1|_{\mathbf{B}} U_{\mathsf{amp},T} |\psi\rangle_{\mathbf{X},\mathbf{Y}} |0\rangle_{\mathbf{B},\mathbf{Anc}} = \sum_{anc} \left|\psi'_{anc}\right\rangle_{\mathbf{X},\mathbf{Y}} |1\rangle_{\mathbf{B}} |anc\rangle_{\mathbf{Anc}}$$

by using sub-normalized states  $|\psi'_{anc}\rangle_{\mathbf{X},\mathbf{Y}}$  that are in the span of  $\Pi(I_{\mathbf{X}}\otimes(|0\rangle\langle 0|)_{\mathbf{Y}})$ .

<span id="page-25-3"></span>(b) (Amplification of success probability in  $S_{\geq \delta}$ .) For any noticeable function  $\nu = \nu(\lambda)$ , there is  $T = \mathsf{poly}(\lambda)$  such that for any quantum state  $|\phi\rangle_{\mathbf{X}} \in \mathcal{H}_{\mathbf{X}}$  s.t.  $|\phi\rangle_{\mathbf{X}} |0\rangle_{\mathbf{Y}} \in S_{\geq \delta}$ , we have

$$\| |1\rangle \langle 1|_{\mathbf{B}} U_{\mathsf{amp},T} |\phi\rangle_{\mathbf{X}} |0\rangle_{\mathbf{Y}} |0\rangle_{\mathbf{B},\mathbf{Anc}} \|^2 \ge 1 - \nu.$$

<span id="page-25-5"></span>(c)  $(S_{<\delta} \text{ and } S_{\geq \delta} \text{ are invariant under } U_{\mathsf{amp},T})$ . For any quantum state  $|\psi_{<\delta}\rangle_{\mathbf{X},\mathbf{Y}} \in S_{<\delta}$  and any b, anc, we can write

$$U_{\mathsf{amp},T} \left| \psi_{<\delta} \right\rangle_{\mathbf{X},\mathbf{Y}} \left| b, anc \right\rangle_{\mathbf{B},\mathbf{Anc}} = \sum_{b',anc'} \left| \psi'_{<\delta,b',anc'} \right\rangle_{\mathbf{X},\mathbf{Y}} \left| b', anc' \right\rangle_{\mathbf{B},\mathbf{Anc}}$$

by using sub-normalized states  $\left|\psi'_{<\delta,b',anc'}\right\rangle_{\mathbf{X},\mathbf{Y}} \in S_{<\delta}$ .

Similarly, for any quantum state  $|\psi_{\geq \delta}\rangle_{\mathbf{X},\mathbf{Y}} \in S_{\geq \delta}$  and any b, anc, we can write

$$U_{\mathsf{amp},T} \left| \psi_{\geq \delta} \right\rangle_{\mathbf{X},\mathbf{Y}} \left| b, anc \right\rangle_{\mathbf{B},\mathbf{Anc}} = \sum_{b',anc'} \left| \psi'_{\geq \delta,b',anc'} \right\rangle_{\mathbf{X},\mathbf{Y}} \left| b', anc' \right\rangle_{\mathbf{B},\mathbf{Anc}}$$

by using sub-normalized states  $\left|\psi'_{\geq\delta,b',anc'}\right\rangle_{\mathbf{X}\mathbf{Y}} \in S_{\geq\delta}$ .

<span id="page-25-4"></span>4. (Efficient Implementation of  $U_{\mathsf{amp},T}$ .) There exists a QPT algorithm  $\mathsf{Amp}$  (whose description is independent of  $\Pi$ ) that takes as input  $1^T$ , a description of quantum circuit that perform a measurement  $(\Pi, I_{\mathbf{X},\mathbf{Y}} - \Pi)$ , and a state  $|\psi\rangle_{\mathbf{X},\mathbf{Y},\mathbf{B},\mathbf{Anc}}$ , and outputs  $U_{\mathsf{amp},T} |\psi\rangle_{\mathbf{X},\mathbf{Y},\mathbf{B},\mathbf{Anc}}$ . Moreover,  $\mathsf{Amp}$  uses the measurement circuit for only implementing an oracle that apply unitary to write a measurement result in a designated register in  $\mathsf{Anc}$ , and it acts on  $\mathbf{X}$  only through the oracle access.

The only difference from [CCY21, Lemma 3.3] is Item 3a where we require that an output of  $U_{\mathsf{amp},T}$  to be in the span of  $\Pi(I_{\mathbf{X}} \otimes (|0\rangle \langle 0|)_{\mathbf{Y}})$  when **B** contains 1 whereas [CCY21, Lemma 3.3] only requires it to be in the span of  $\Pi$ .<sup>25</sup> We can prove Lem. 10 by using Jordan's lemma in a very similar manner to that for the proof of [CCY21, Lemma 3.3]. Thus, we defer the proof to Appx. B.1.

#### <span id="page-25-0"></span>4.4 Proof of Lem. 6

By using Lem. 3 and 8 to 10, we prove Lem. 6, which completes the proof of Lem. 4.

Proof of Lem. 6. First, we define a "simulation-less extractor"  $\mathsf{Ext}_{\mathsf{Sim-less}}$  that works as follows:  $\mathsf{Ext}_{\mathsf{Sim-less}}(1^{\lambda}, \{\Pi_i\}_{i \in C}, \mathcal{A}, |\psi\rangle)$ :

<span id="page-25-2"></span>In the previous version of this paper, we only required the state to be in the span of  $\Pi$  similarly to [CCY21, Lemma 3.3]. However, we found that we needed the above stronger requirement due to a technical reason. In particular, this is used in the proof of Claim 4.

{26}------------------------------------------------

- 1. Uniformly choose  $(i, j) \leftarrow C^2$  and immediately output Fail if  $(i, j) \notin S$ .
- 2. For  $k \in \{i, j\}$  in the order of k = j, k = i, on the following:
  - (a) Perform the measurement  $\{\Pi_k, I \Pi_k\}$ , and immediately output Fail if the state is projected onto  $I \Pi_k$ . Otherwise, go to the next step with the residual state  $|\psi'_k\rangle$ . <sup>27</sup>
  - (b) Run  $s_i \leftarrow \mathcal{A}_0(k, |\psi_k'\rangle)$  in a non-destructive way, i.e., in a way such that the state  $|\psi_k'\rangle$  is preserved. This is possible since the output of  $\mathcal{A}_0$  is deterministic as required in Item 2 of Lem. 4.
- 3. Run  $s \leftarrow \mathcal{A}_1(i, j, s_i, s_j)$  and output s.

Then, by combining Lem. 3 and Item 3 of Lem. 4, we can show the following claim.

<span id="page-26-2"></span>Claim 2. For any  $|\psi\rangle \in \mathcal{H}$ ,  $\operatorname{Ext}_{\mathsf{Sim-less}}(1^{\lambda}, \{\Pi_i\}_{i \in C}, \mathcal{A}, |\psi\rangle)$  outputs  $s^*$  whenever it does not output Fail and it holds that

$$\Pr\left[\mathsf{Ext}_{\mathsf{Sim-less}}(1^{\lambda}, \{\Pi_i\}_{i \in C}, \mathcal{A}, |\psi\rangle) = s^*\right] \geq \left(\sum_{i \in C} \frac{1}{|C|} \|\Pi_i |\psi\rangle\|^2\right)^3 - \left(1 - \frac{|S|}{|C|^2}\right).$$

Proof of Claim 2. The former part is clear from Item 3 of Lem. 4. For the latter part, we only have to lower bound the probability that  $\mathsf{Ext}_{\mathsf{Sim-less}}$  does not return Fail. If we remove the condition for outputting Fail in the first step of  $\mathsf{Ext}_{\mathsf{Sim-less}}$ , it is clear that the probability to not output Fail is  $\sum_{i,j\in C^2} \frac{1}{|C|^2} \|\Pi_i\Pi_j\|\psi\rangle\|^2$ , which is lower bounded by  $\left(\sum_{i\in C} \frac{1}{|C|} \|\Pi_i\|\psi\rangle\|^2\right)^3$  by Lem. 3. Moreover, it is easy to see that the probability to output Fail in the first step of  $\mathsf{Ext}_{\mathsf{Sim-less}}$  is  $\frac{|S|}{|C|^2}$ . By union bound, the latter part of the claim follows.

Our next step is to apply Lem. 10 with respect to a projection corresponding to the success of  $\mathsf{Ext}_{\mathsf{Sim-less}}$ . Let  $U_{\mathsf{Sim-less}}$  be the unitary that represents  $\mathsf{Ext}_{\mathsf{Sim-less}}(1^{\lambda}, \{\Pi_i\}_{i \in C}, \mathcal{A}, \cdot)$ . More precisely, we define  $U_{\mathsf{Sim-less}}$  over registers the input register  $\mathsf{Inp}$ , working register  $\mathbf{W}$ , and output register  $\mathsf{Out}$  so that  $\mathsf{Ext}_{\mathsf{Sim-less}}(1^{\lambda}, \{\Pi_i\}_{i \in C}, \mathcal{A}, \cdot)$  can be described as follows:

 $\operatorname{Ext}_{\operatorname{Sim-less}}(1^{\lambda}, \{\Pi_i\}_{i \in C}, \mathcal{A}, \cdot))$ : It takes a quantum state  $|\psi\rangle$  in the register  $\operatorname{Inp}$  and initializes registers  $\mathbf{W}$  and  $\operatorname{Out}$  to be  $|0\rangle_{\mathbf{W}, \mathbf{Out}}$ . Then it applies the unitary  $U_{\operatorname{Sim-less}}$ , measures the register  $\operatorname{Out}$  in the standard basis to obtain s, and outputs s.

We define a projection  $\Pi$  over (lnp, W, Out) as

$$\Pi := U_{\mathsf{Sim-less}}^{\dagger} \left( \sum_{s \neq \mathsf{Fail}} |s\rangle \langle s| \right) U_{\mathsf{Sim-less}}.$$
(6)

Then the following claim immediately follows from the former half of Claim 2.

<span id="page-26-3"></span>Claim 3. Given any state in the span of  $\Pi(I_{\mathsf{Inp}} \otimes (|0\rangle \langle 0|)_{\mathbf{W},\mathbf{Out}})$ , if we apply  $U_{\mathsf{Sim\text{-}less}}$  and then measure register  $\mathbf{Out}$ , then the measurement outcome is always  $s^*$ 

We apply Lem. 10 for the above  $\Pi$  where  $\mathcal{H}_{\mathbf{X}} := \mathcal{H}_{\mathsf{Inp}}$ ,  $\mathcal{H}_{\mathbf{Y}} := \mathcal{H}_{\mathbf{W}} \otimes \mathcal{H}_{\mathbf{Out}}$ ,  $\delta := \left(\frac{\varepsilon}{5}\right)^{12} - \left(1 - \frac{|S|}{|C|^2}\right)$ , and  $T = \mathsf{poly}(\lambda)$  is chosen in such a way that Item 3b of Lem. 10 holds for  $\nu := \frac{\varepsilon^4}{16}$ . Then we have a decomposition  $(S_{<\delta}, S_{\geq \delta})$  of  $\mathcal{H}_{\mathbf{X}} \otimes \mathcal{H}_{\mathbf{Y}}$  and a unitary  $U_{\mathsf{amp},T}$  over  $\mathcal{H}_{\mathbf{X}} \otimes \mathcal{H}_{\mathbf{Y}} \otimes \mathcal{H}_{\mathbf{B}} \otimes \mathcal{H}_{\mathbf{Anc}}$  that satisfy

<span id="page-26-0"></span><sup>&</sup>lt;sup>26</sup> Whichever order is fine for our purpose. We specify it just for completeness of the description of the algorithm.

<span id="page-26-1"></span>Note that  $|\psi'_j\rangle = \frac{\Pi_j |\psi\rangle}{|\Pi_j |\psi\rangle|}$  and  $|\psi'_i\rangle = \frac{\Pi_i \Pi_j |\psi\rangle}{|\Pi_i \Pi_j |\psi\rangle|}$ 

{27}------------------------------------------------

<span id="page-27-3"></span>the requirements in [Lem. 10.](#page-24-1) We denote by Other to mean the registers W, Out, B, and Anc for brevity. We construct the extractor ExtSim,na for [Lem. 6](#page-22-1) as follows:

$$\mathsf{Ext}_{\mathsf{Sim},\mathsf{na}}(1^{\lambda},1^{\varepsilon^{-1}},\{\Pi_i\}_{i\in C},\mathcal{A},|\psi_{\mathsf{init}}\rangle)$$
:

- 1. Set |ψiniti in register Inp and initlialize register Other to be |0i.
- 2. Apply Uamp,T by using the algorithm Amp in [Item 4](#page-25-4) of [Lem. 10.](#page-24-1)
- <span id="page-27-2"></span>3. Measure register B and let b be the outcome. If b = 0, output Fail and immediately halt. Otherwise, proceed to the next step.
- <span id="page-27-1"></span>4. Apply USim-less, measure register Out to obtain an outcome sExt, and apply U † Sim-less.
- 5. Apply U † amp,T by using the algorithm Amp in [Item 4](#page-25-4) of [Lem. 10.](#page-24-1)
- 6. Measure register Other. If the outcome is not the all 0's string, output Fail and immediately halt. Otherwise, let |ψmidi be the state in register Inp at this point, and proceed to the next step.
- 7. Choose i ← C.
- 8. Apply the measurement {Π<sup>i</sup> , I − Πi} on |ψmidi.
  - If the state is projected onto Π<sup>i</sup> , output <sup>i</sup>, the classical string <sup>s</sup>Ext and the resulting state <sup>Π</sup>i|ψmid<sup>i</sup> <sup>|</sup>Πi|ψmidi| .
  - If the state is projected onto I − Π<sup>i</sup> , output Fail.

We can easily see the following claim:

<span id="page-27-0"></span>Claim 4. Whenever Step [4](#page-27-1) of ExtSim,na is invoked, sExt obtained in the step is always equal to s ∗ . Moreover, the step does not change the state in registers Inp and Other, that is, the states before and after the step are identical.

Proof of [Claim 4.](#page-27-0) Whenever Step [4](#page-27-1) is invoked, the bit b obtained in Step [3](#page-27-2) is equal to 1. In this case, by [Item 3a](#page-25-1) of [Lem. 10,](#page-24-1) the state in registers Inp, W, and Out is in the span of Π(IInp ⊗(|0i h0|)W,Out). Then, [Claim 3](#page-26-3) implies that sExt is always equal to s ∗ . Then the measurement of Out does not collapse the state and thus the step does not change the state.

The rest of the proof is similar to that of [\[CCY21,](#page-58-7) Claim 4.5]. Let R be an operator defined as follows:

$$R := (|0\rangle \langle 0|)_{\mathbf{Other}} U_{\mathsf{amp},T}^{\dagger} (|1\rangle \langle 1|)_{\mathbf{B}} U_{\mathsf{amp},T}.$$

Let Π<δ and Π≥<sup>δ</sup> be projections onto S<δ and S≥<sup>δ</sup> , respectively. To apply Lemma [8,](#page-24-2) we define states |φ0i = |φ0,0i + |φ0,1i and |φ1i = |φ1,0i + |φ1,1i over (D, Inp, Other) where D is an additional one-qubit register as follows:

$$\begin{split} |\phi_{0}\rangle &:= |1\rangle_{\mathbf{D}} \, |\psi_{\mathsf{init}}\rangle_{\mathsf{Inp}} \, |0\rangle_{\mathbf{Other}} \,, \\ |\phi_{0,0}\rangle &:= |1\rangle_{\mathbf{D}} \, \Pi_{<\delta} \, |\psi_{\mathsf{init}}\rangle_{\mathsf{Inp}} \, |0\rangle_{\mathbf{Other}} \,, \\ |\phi_{0,1}\rangle &:= |1\rangle_{\mathbf{D}} \, \Pi_{\geq\delta} \, |\psi_{\mathsf{init}}\rangle_{\mathsf{Inp}} \, |0\rangle_{\mathbf{Other}} \,, \\ |\phi_{1}\rangle &:= |1\rangle_{\mathbf{D}} \, R \, |\psi_{\mathsf{init}}\rangle_{\mathsf{Inp}} \, |0\rangle_{\mathbf{Other}} + \alpha \, |0\rangle_{\mathbf{D}} \, |0\rangle_{\mathsf{Inp}} \, |0\rangle_{\mathbf{Other}} \,, \\ |\phi_{1,0}\rangle &:= |1\rangle_{\mathbf{D}} \, R \, \Pi_{<\delta} \, |\psi_{\mathsf{init}}\rangle_{\mathsf{Inp}} \, |0\rangle_{\mathbf{Other}} + \alpha \, |0\rangle_{\mathbf{D}} \, |0\rangle_{\mathsf{Inp}} \, |0\rangle_{\mathbf{Other}} \,, \\ |\phi_{1,1}\rangle &:= |1\rangle_{\mathbf{D}} \, R \, \Pi_{\geq\delta} \, |\psi_{\mathsf{init}}\rangle_{\mathsf{Inp}} \, |0\rangle_{\mathbf{Other}} \,. \end{split}$$

for α := q 1 − kR |ψiniti Inp |0iOther k <sup>2</sup> (so that |φ1i is a normalized state). Let F be a quantum algorithm that works as follows:

{28}------------------------------------------------

 $F\left(|\phi\rangle_{\mathbf{D},\mathsf{Inp},\mathbf{Other}}\right)$ : It measures  $\mathbf{D}$ , and outputs Fail if the outcome is 0. Otherwise, it samples  $i \leftarrow C$  and applies the measurement  $\{\Pi_i, I - \Pi_i\}$  on register  $\mathsf{Inp}$ . If the state is projected onto  $\Pi_i$ , output i, the measurement outcome s of the second register, and the resulting state in the third register. Otherwise, it outputs Fail.

It is easy to see that

$$\operatorname{Exp}_{\mathsf{na}}(\lambda, \{\Pi_i\}_{i \in C}, |\psi_{\mathsf{init}}\rangle) \equiv F(|\phi_0\rangle \langle \phi_0|).$$

Moreover, by the definition of Ext<sub>Sim,na</sub> and Claim 4, we can see that

$$\operatorname{Ext}_{\operatorname{Sim.na}}(1^{\lambda}, 1^{\varepsilon^{-1}}, \{\Pi_i\}_{i \in C}, \mathcal{A}, |\psi_{\operatorname{init}}\rangle) \equiv F(|\phi_1\rangle \langle \phi_1|).$$

Thus, it suffices to prove that the distinguishing advantage between  $F(|\phi_0\rangle \langle \phi_0|)$  and  $F(|\phi_1\rangle \langle \phi_1|)$  is at most  $\varepsilon$ . To apply Lem. 8, we prove the following claim.

<span id="page-28-0"></span>Claim 5. The following hold:

1. 
$$\Pr\left[F\left(\frac{|\phi_{b,0}\rangle\langle\phi_{b,0}|}{\||\phi_{b,0}\rangle\|^2}\right) = \mathsf{Fail}\right] \ge 1 - \left(\frac{\varepsilon}{5}\right)^4 \ for \ b \in \{0,1\}.$$

2. 
$$\| |\phi_{1,1}\rangle - |\phi_{0,1}\rangle \| \leq \left(\frac{\varepsilon}{2}\right)^2$$
.

Proof of Claim 5.

First item. We can write  $\Pi_{<\delta} |\psi_{\mathsf{init}}\rangle_{\mathsf{Inp}} |0\rangle_{\mathsf{Other}} = |\psi_{<\delta}\rangle_{\mathsf{Inp}} |0\rangle_{\mathsf{Other}}$ . Then we have

$$\begin{split} \Pr \bigg[ F \left( \frac{|\phi_{0,0}\rangle \left\langle \phi_{0,0}| \right|}{\| \left\| \phi_{0,0} \right\rangle \|^2} \right) \neq \mathsf{Fail} \bigg] &= \sum_{i \in C} \frac{1}{|C|} \left\| \Pi_i \frac{|\psi_{<\delta}\rangle}{\| \left\| \psi_{<\delta} \right\|} \right\|^2 \\ &\leq \left( \Pr \bigg[ \mathsf{Ext}_{\mathsf{Sim-less}} \left( 1^\lambda, \left\{ \Pi_i \right\}_{i \in C}, \mathcal{A}, \frac{|\psi_{<\delta}\rangle}{\| \left\| \psi_{<\delta} \right\rangle \|} \right) = s^* \bigg] + \left( 1 - \frac{|S|}{|C|^2} \right) \right)^{1/3} \\ &\leq \left( \delta + \left( 1 - \frac{|S|}{|C|^2} \right) \right)^{1/3} \\ &= \left( \frac{\varepsilon}{5} \right)^4 \end{split}$$

where the first inequality follows from Claim 2, the second inequality follows from  $|\psi_{<\delta}\rangle_{\mathsf{Inp}}|0\rangle_{\mathsf{Other}} \in S_{<\delta}$  and Item 2 of Lem. 10, and the final equality follows from  $\delta = \left(\frac{\varepsilon}{5}\right)^{12} - \left(1 - \frac{|S|}{|C|^2}\right)$ . This completes the proof of the first item for the case of b = 0. The case of b = 1 can be proven similarly noting that  $R\Pi_{<\delta} |\psi_{\mathsf{init}}\rangle_{\mathsf{Inp}} |0\rangle_{\mathsf{Other}} \in S_{<\delta}$  by Item 1 and item 3c of Lemma 10.

**Second Item.** By Item 3b of Lem. 10, we have

$$\|(|1\rangle\langle 1|)_{\mathbf{B}}U_{\mathsf{amp},T}\Pi_{\geq t}|\psi_{\mathsf{init}}\rangle_{\mathsf{Inp}}|0\rangle_{\mathbf{Other}}\|^{2}\leq \nu.$$

Thus, Lem. 9 implies

$$\|\Pi_{\geq t} |\psi_{\mathsf{init}}\rangle_{\mathsf{Inp}} |0\rangle_{\mathbf{Other}} - R\Pi_{\geq t} |\psi_{\mathsf{init}}\rangle_{\mathsf{Inp}} |0\rangle_{\mathbf{Other}} \| \leq \nu^{1/2}.$$

Since  $\nu = \frac{\varepsilon^4}{16}$ , this immediately implies the second item of the claim.

By Lem. 8 and Claim 5 the distinguishing advantage between  $F(|\phi_0\rangle \langle \phi_0|)$  and  $F(|\phi_1\rangle \langle \phi_1|)$  is at most  $\left(12\left(\frac{\varepsilon}{5}\right)^2 + 2\left(\frac{\varepsilon}{2}\right)^2\right)^{1/2} < \varepsilon$ . This completes the proof of Lem. 6.

{29}------------------------------------------------

#### <span id="page-29-6"></span><span id="page-29-0"></span>5 Black-Box $\varepsilon$ -Simulation-Extractable Commitments in Constant Rounds

In this section, we construct a post-quantum commitment scheme that satisfies the (parallel) strong extractability with  $\varepsilon$ -simulation. Namely, we prove the following lemma.

**Lemma 11.** Assume the existence of post-quantum secure OWFs. Then, there exists a constant-round construction of post-quantum commitment that satisfies computational hiding (Def. 8), statistical binding (Def. 9), and (parallel) strongly extractable commitment with  $\varepsilon$ -simulation (Def. 11 and 12). Moreover, this construction makes only black-box use of the assumed OWF.

Toward proving that, we first construct a scheme that satisfies a weaker notion of  $\varepsilon$ -simulatable extractability in Sec. 5.1. In Sec. 5.2, we present a compiler that converts the weak scheme in Sec. 5.1 into one that satisfies the (parallel) strong extractability with  $\varepsilon$ -simulation.

### <span id="page-29-1"></span>5.1 Weakly Extractable Commitment

We construct a commitment scheme that satisfies weak notions of extractability defined in Def. 13 and 16 based on OWFs. The description of the scheme is given in Prot. 1, where Com is a statistically-binding and computationally-hiding commitment scheme (e.g., Naor's commitment). We remark that the scheme is identical to the *classical* extractable commitment in [PW09], which in turn is based on earlier works [DDN00, PRS02, Ros04].

#### <span id="page-29-2"></span>Protocol 1: Extractable Commitment Scheme wExtCom

The extractable commitment scheme, based on any commitment scheme Com, works in the following way. Input:

- both the committer C and the receiver R get security parameter  $1^{\lambda}$  as the common input.
- C gets a string  $m \in \{0,1\}^{\ell(\lambda)}$  as his private input, where  $\ell(\cdot)$  is a polynomial

#### Commitment Phase:

- <span id="page-29-4"></span>1. The committer C commits using  $\mathsf{Com}$  to  $k = \lambda$  pairs of strings  $\{(v_i^0, v_i^1)\}_{i=1}^k$  where  $(v_i^0, v_i^1) = (\eta_i, m \oplus \eta_i)$  and  $\eta_i$  are random strings in  $\{0, 1\}^\ell$  for  $1 \le i \le k$ . We denote those commitments by  $\overline{\mathsf{com}} = \{\mathsf{com}_i^0, \mathsf{com}_i^1\}_{i=1}^k$ .
- <span id="page-29-5"></span>2. Upon receiving a challenge  $\mathbf{c} = (c_1, \dots, c_k)$  from the receiver R, S opens the commitments to  $\mathbf{v} := (v_1^{c_1}, \dots, v_k^{c_k})$  with the corresponding decommitment  $\overline{\mathsf{decom}} := (\mathsf{decom}_1^{c_1}, \dots, \mathsf{decom}_k^{c_k})$ .
- 3. R checks that the openings are valid.

#### **Decommitment Phase:**

- C sends  $\sigma$  and opens the commitments to all k pairs of strings. R checks that all the openings are valid, and also that  $m = v_1^0 \oplus v_1^1 = \cdots = v_k^0 \oplus v_k^1$ .

**Proof of Security.** The correctness and the statistically-binding property of wExtCom follows straightforwardly from that of Com. The computationally-hiding property of wExtCom can be reduced to that of Com by standard arguments.

## Lemma 12 (Computational Hiding). wExtCom is computationally hiding.

Proof (sketch). Since this can be proven similarly to the classical counterpart in [PW09], we only give a proof sketch. For messages  $m_0, m_1$  and  $j \in [k+1]$ , we consider a hybrid  $\mathsf{Hyb}_j$  where  $(v_i^0, v_i^1)$  are 2-out-of-2 secret shares of  $m_0$  for  $i \geq j$  and those of  $m_1$  for  $i \leq j-1$ . What we should show is that  $\mathsf{Hyb}_0$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{k+1}$  are computationally indistinguishable from the view of a malicious receiver. By a standard hybrid argument, it suffices to prove the computational indistinguishability between  $\mathsf{Hyb}_j$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb}_{j+1}$ . This can be reduced to the computational hiding property of  $\mathsf{Com}$  by guessing  $c_j$  and embedding the

<span id="page-29-3"></span>Actually, the scheme will be secure as long as we use Com to commit  $k = \omega(\log \lambda)$  pairs of strings.

{30}------------------------------------------------

instance of computational hiding of Com into  $\mathsf{com}_j^{1-c_j}$ . The reduction works as long as the guess is correct, which occurs with probability 1/2.

We prove that wExtCom satisfies a weak version of the extractability which we call the weak extractability with  $\varepsilon$ -simulation. Intuitively, it requires the simulation-extractor to perform extraction and  $\varepsilon$ -simulation properly, as long as the commitment is valid. A formal definition is given below.

**Definition 13 (Weak Extractability with** ε-Simulation). A commitment scheme  $\Pi$  is weakly extractable with ε-simulation if there exists a QPT algorithm  $\mathcal{SE}_{\mathsf{weak}}$  (called the ε-simulation weak-extractor) such that for any noticeable  $\varepsilon(\lambda)$  and any non-uniform QPT  $C^*(\rho)$ ,

<span id="page-30-0"></span>
$$\begin{split} & \big\{ \varGamma_{\mathsf{com}}(m_{\mathsf{Ext}}, \widetilde{\mathsf{ST}}_{C^*}) : (\mathsf{com}, m_{\mathsf{Ext}}, \widetilde{\mathsf{ST}}_{C^*}) \leftarrow \mathcal{SE}^{C^*(\rho)}(1^{\lambda}, 1^{\varepsilon^{-1}}) \big\}_{\lambda} \\ & \stackrel{c}{\approx}_{\varepsilon} \big\{ \varGamma_{\mathsf{com}}(\mathsf{val}_{\Pi}(\mathsf{com}), \mathsf{ST}_{C^*}) : (\mathsf{com}, \mathsf{ST}_{C^*}, b_{\mathsf{com}}) \leftarrow \langle C^*(\rho), R \rangle (1^{\lambda}) \big\}_{\lambda} \end{split}$$

$$\textit{where } \Gamma_{\mathsf{com}}(m,\mathsf{ST}_{C^*}) \coloneqq \begin{cases} (m,\mathsf{ST}_{C^*}) & \textit{if } \mathsf{val}_\Pi(\mathsf{com}) \neq \bot \\ \bot & \textit{otherwise} \end{cases}.$$

<span id="page-30-1"></span>Lemma 13 (Weak Extractability with  $\varepsilon$ -Simulation). wExtCom is weakly extractable with  $\varepsilon$ -simulation (as per Def. 13).

Before proving Lem. 13, we prepare several definitions.

<span id="page-30-2"></span>**Definition 14 (Validness of**  $\overline{\text{com}}$ ). For a sequence  $\overline{\text{com}} = \{\text{com}_i^0, \text{com}_i^1\}_{i=1}^k$  of commitments of the scheme Com, we say that  $\overline{\text{com}}$  is valid if there exists  $m \in \{0,1\}^\ell$  such that  $\text{val}_{\mathsf{Com}}(\mathsf{com}_i^b) \neq \bot$  for all  $i \in [k]$  and  $b \in \{0,1\}$  and  $\text{val}_{\mathsf{Com}}(\mathsf{com}_i^0) \oplus \text{val}_{\mathsf{Com}}(\mathsf{com}_i^1) = m$  for all  $i \in [k]$  where  $\text{val}_{\mathsf{Com}}(\mathsf{com}_i^b)$  is the value function as defined in  $\overline{\mathsf{Def}}$ . 10. We denote by  $\text{val}_{\mathsf{Com}}(\overline{\mathsf{com}})$  to mean such m if  $\overline{\mathsf{com}}$  is valid and otherwise  $\bot$ .

<span id="page-30-3"></span>**Definition 15 (Accepting Opening of**  $\overline{\text{com}}$ ). For a sequence  $\overline{\text{com}} = \{\text{com}_i^0, \text{com}_i^1\}_{i=1}^k$  of commitments of the commitment scheme  $\text{Com } and \mathbf{c} = (c_1, ..., c_k) \in \{0, 1\}^k$ , we say that  $(\mathbf{v} = (v_1, ..., v_k), \overline{\text{decom}} = (\text{decom}_1, ..., \text{decom}_k))$  is an accepting opening of  $\overline{\text{com}}$  w.r.t.  $\mathbf{c}$  if  $\text{Verify}_{\text{Com}}(\text{com}_i^{c_i}, v_i, \text{decom}_i) = 1$  for all  $i \in [k]$ .

Then we prove Lem. 13.

*Proof of Lem. 13.* For simplicity, we assume that Com satisfies perfect binding. It is straightforward to extend the proof to the statistically binding case by excluding the bad case where any commitment of Com is not bounded to a unique message, which happens with a negligible probability.

Remark that the weak extractability with  $\varepsilon$ -simulation only requires the extractor to correctly extract and simulate if the commitment generated in the commit stage is valid in the sense of Def. 10. When the commitment is valid,  $\overline{\mathsf{com}}$  generated in Step 1 is also valid in the sense of Def. 14 (because otherwise a committer cannot pass the verification in the decommitment stage). Therefore, it suffices to prove that the extractor works for any fixed valid  $\overline{\mathsf{com}}$ .

Let  $C^*(\rho)$  be a non-uniform QPT malicious committer. For  $\mathbf{c} \in \{0,1\}^k$ , let  $U_{\mathbf{c}}$  be the unitary corresponding to the action of  $C^*$  in Step 2. That is, for the state  $\rho'$  before Step 2, it applies  $U_{\mathbf{c}}$  to  $\underline{\text{get } U_{\mathbf{c}}\rho'U_{\mathbf{c}}^{\dagger}}$  and measures designated registers  $\mathbf{V}$  and  $\mathbf{D}$  to get the message  $\mathbf{v}$  and opening information  $\underline{\text{decom}}$  in Step 2. Let  $\Pi_{\mathbf{c}}^{\mathsf{test}}$  be the projection that maps onto states that contain an accepting opening  $\mathbf{v}$  and  $\underline{\text{decom}}$  of  $\underline{\text{com}}$  w.r.t.  $\mathbf{c}$  (as defined in Def. 15) in  $\mathbf{V} \otimes \mathbf{D}$ . For  $\mathbf{c} \in \{0,1\}^k$ , we define  $\Pi_{\mathbf{c}} := U_{\mathbf{c}}^{\dagger} \Pi_{\mathbf{c}}^{\mathsf{test}} U_{\mathbf{c}}$ .

We apply Lem. 4 for  $\{\Pi_{\mathbf{c}}\}_{\mathbf{c}\in\{0,1\}^k}$  with the following correspondence.

 $-\mathcal{H}$  is the internal space of  $C^*$ .

{31}------------------------------------------------

- The initial state is  $\rho'$ .<sup>29</sup>
- $C = \{0, 1\}^k.$
- $-S = \{((c_1, ..., c_k), (c'_1, ..., c'_k)) : \exists i \in [k] \text{ s.t. } c_i \neq c'_i\}$
- $-\mathcal{A}_0$  applies  $U_{\mathbf{c}}$  on its input, measures  $\mathbf{V}$  to get  $\mathbf{v}$ , applies  $U_{\mathbf{c}}^{\dagger}$ , and outputs  $\mathbf{v}$ .
- $\mathcal{A}_1$  is given as input  $(\mathbf{c}, \mathbf{c}') \in S$ ,  $\mathbf{v_c} = (v_1^{c_1}, ..., v_k^{c_k})$ , and  $\mathbf{v_{c'}} = (v_1^{c'_1}, ..., v_k^{c'_k})$ .  $\mathcal{A}_1$  outputs  $v_i^{c_i} \oplus v_i^{c'_i}$  for the smallest  $i \in [k]$  such that  $c_i \neq c'_i$ . Note that such i exists since we assume  $(\mathbf{c}, \mathbf{c}') \in S$ .

If  $\overline{\mathsf{com}}$  is valid, we can see that the assumptions for Lem. 4 are satisfied as follows:

- 1. By the definition of S, it is easy to see that  $\frac{|S|}{|C|^2} = 1 2^{-k} = 1 \text{negl}(\lambda)$ .
- 2. For any  $\mathbf{c}$ , if  $\mathcal{A}_0$  takes a state in the span of  $\Pi_{\mathbf{c}}$  as input, it outputs  $s_{\mathbf{c}} := (\mathsf{val}_{\mathsf{Com}}(\mathsf{com}_1^{c_1}), ..., \mathsf{val}_{\mathsf{Com}}(\mathsf{com}_k^{c_k}))$  with probability 1 by the definition of  $\Pi_{\mathbf{c}}$  and the perfect binding property of  $\mathsf{Com}$ .
- 3. For any  $(\mathbf{c}, \mathbf{c}') \in S$ , if  $\mathcal{A}_1$  takes as input the  $s_{\mathbf{c}}$  and  $s_{\mathbf{c}'}$  defined as follows:

$$\begin{cases} s_{\mathbf{c}} = (\mathsf{val}_{\mathsf{Com}}(\mathsf{com}_1^{c_1}), \dots, \mathsf{val}_{\mathsf{Com}}(\mathsf{com}_k^{c_k})) \\ s_{\mathbf{c}'} = (\mathsf{val}_{\mathsf{Com}}(\mathsf{com}_1^{c_1'}), \dots, \mathsf{val}_{\mathsf{Com}}(\mathsf{com}_k^{c_k'})) \end{cases};$$

then, it outputs  $s^* := \mathsf{val}_{\mathsf{Com}}(\overline{\mathsf{com}})$  as defined in Def. 14 since we assume that  $\overline{\mathsf{com}}$  is valid.

Let  $\widetilde{\mathcal{SE}}$  be the  $\varepsilon$ -simulation extractor of Lem. 4 in the above setting. Then Lem. 4 gives us the following:

$$\{\widetilde{\mathcal{SE}}(1^{\lambda},1^{\varepsilon^{-1}},\{\Pi_{\mathbf{c}}\}_{\mathbf{c}\in\{0,1\}^{k}},\mathcal{A},\rho')\}_{\lambda}\overset{s}{\approx}_{\varepsilon}\{\operatorname{Exp}(\lambda,\{\Pi_{\mathbf{c}}\}_{\mathbf{c}\in\{0,1\}^{k}},\rho')\}_{\lambda}$$

where  $\mathsf{Exp}(\lambda, \{\Pi_{\mathbf{c}}\}_{\mathbf{c}\in\{0,1\}^k}, \rho')$  is as defined in Lem. 4. That is,  $\mathsf{Exp}(\lambda, \{\Pi_{\mathbf{c}}\}_{\mathbf{c}\in\{0,1\}^k}, \rho')$  works as follows:

- Choose  $\mathbf{c} \leftarrow \{0,1\}^k$ .
- Apply the measurement  $\{\Pi_{\mathbf{c}}, I \Pi_{\mathbf{c}}\}$  on  $\rho'$ .
  - If the state is projected onto  $\Pi_{\mathbf{c}}$ , the experiment outputs  $\mathbf{c}$ , the classical string  $\mathsf{val}_{\mathsf{Com}}(\overline{\mathsf{com}})$ , and the resulting state.
  - If the state is projected onto  $I \Pi_c$ , the experiment outputs  $c, \perp$ , and the resulting state.

One can see that the state in the third output of  $\operatorname{Exp}(\lambda, \rho')$  is similar to the final state of  $C^*$  in the real execution except that  $C^*$  applies the unitary  $U_{\mathbf{c}}$  instead of the measurement  $\{\Pi_{\mathbf{c}}, I - \Pi_{\mathbf{c}}\}$  and measures  $\mathbf{V}$  and  $\mathbf{D}$ . By noting that  $\Pi_{\mathbf{c}}^{\mathsf{test}}U_{\mathbf{c}} = U_{\mathbf{c}}\Pi_{\mathbf{c}}$  and that measuring  $\mathbf{V}$  and  $\mathbf{D}$  is the same as first applying the measurement  $\{\Pi_{\mathbf{c}}^{\mathsf{test}}, I - \Pi_{\mathbf{c}}^{\mathsf{test}}\}$  and then measuring  $\mathbf{V}$  and  $\mathbf{D}$ , if we apply  $U_{\mathbf{c}}$  on the third output of  $\operatorname{Exp}(\lambda, \{\Pi_{\mathbf{c}}\}_{\mathbf{c} \in \{0,1\}^k}, \rho')$  and then measure  $\mathbf{V}$  and  $\mathbf{D}$ , the state is exactly the same as the final state of  $C^*$ .

Therefore, the following extractor  $\mathcal{SE}_{\mathsf{weak}}$  works for the weak  $\varepsilon$ -simulation extractability:  $\mathcal{SE}_{\mathsf{weak}}^{C^*(\rho)}(1^{\lambda}, 1^{\varepsilon^{-1}})$ :

- 1. Run the commit stage of wExtCom between  $C^*(\rho)$  and the honest receiver R until  $C^*$  sends  $\overline{\text{com}}$  in Step 1. Let  $\rho'$  be the internal state of  $C^*$  at this point.
- 2. Run  $(\mathbf{c}, m_{\mathsf{Ext}}, \rho_{\mathsf{Ext}}) \leftarrow \widetilde{\mathcal{SE}}(1^{\lambda}, 1^{\varepsilon^{-1}}, \{\Pi_{\mathbf{c}}\}_{\mathbf{c} \in \{0,1\}^k}, \mathcal{A}, \rho')$  where  $\mathcal{A} = (\mathcal{A}_0, \mathcal{A}_1)$  is as defined above. Remark that the definition of  $\Pi_{\mathbf{c}}$  depends on  $\overline{\mathsf{com}}$ , and it uses  $\overline{\mathsf{com}}$  generated in the previous step.

<span id="page-31-0"></span>Though we assume that the initial state  $|\psi_{\text{init}}\rangle$  is a pure state in Lem. 4, the lemma holds for any mixed state since a mixed state can be seen as a probability distribution over pure states.

{32}------------------------------------------------

3. Apply  $U_{\mathbf{c}}$  on  $\rho_{\mathsf{Ext}}$  to generate  $U_{\mathbf{c}}\rho_{\mathsf{Ext}}U_{\mathbf{c}}^{\dagger}$  and measures registers  $\mathbf{V}$  and  $\mathbf{D}$  to get  $\mathbf{v}$  and  $\overline{\mathsf{decom}}$ . Let  $\rho_{\mathsf{final}}$  be the state after the measurement.

4. Output  $(m_{\mathsf{Ext}}, \rho_{\mathsf{final}})$ .

On the Parallel Execution of wExtCom. We can prove that wExtCom satisfies a parallel version of the weak extractability with  $\varepsilon$ -simulation in a similar way. In the following, we prove that wExtCom satisfies even a generalized version of that, which we call *special parallel weak extractability with*  $\varepsilon$ -simulation. Looking ahead, this will be used in the proof of the (parallel)  $\varepsilon$ -simulation strong extractability of Prot. 2 in Sec. 5.2.

Intuitively, it requires the following: Suppose that a malicious committer  $C^*$  interacts with n copies of the honest receiver R in parallel, and let  $\mathsf{com}_j$  be the commitment generated in the j-th execution. Suppose that  $\mathsf{com}_j$  is valid for all  $j \in V$  for some subset  $V \subseteq [n]$ . Let  $F : \{0,1\}^{\ell} \cup \{\bot\} \to \{0,1\}^*$  be a function that is determined by  $\{\mathsf{val}(\mathsf{com}_j)\}_{j \in V}$ , i.e.,  $F(m_1, ..., m_n)$  takes a unique value  $m^*$  as long as  $m_j = \mathsf{val}(\mathsf{com}_j)$  for all  $j \in V$ . Then, the extractor can extract  $m^*$  while simulating the post-execution state of  $C^*$ . A formal definition is given below.

<span id="page-32-0"></span>Definition 16 (Special Parallel Weak Extractability with  $\varepsilon$ -Simulation ). We say that a commitment scheme  $\Pi$  satisfies the special parallel weak extractability with  $\varepsilon$ -simulation if the following is satisfied. For any integer  $n = \text{poly}(\lambda)$  and an efficiently computable function  $F : \{\{0,1\}^{\ell} \cup \{\bot\}\}^n \to \{0,1\}^*$ , there exists  $\mathcal{SE}_F$  that satisfies the following: For commitments  $\{\text{com}_j\}_{j=1}^n$ , we say that  $\{\text{com}_j\}_{j=1}^n$  is F-good if it satisfies the following:

- 1. there exists  $V \subseteq [n]$  such that  $com_j$  is valid (i.e.,  $val_{\Pi}(com_j) \neq \bot$ ) for all  $j \in V$ ; and
- 2. there exists a unique  $m^*$  such that  $F(m'_1,...,m'_n) = m^*$  for all  $(m'_1,...,m'_n)$  such that  $m'_j = \mathsf{val}_\Pi(\mathsf{com}_j)$  for all  $j \in V$ .

Then it holds that

$$\begin{split} &\left\{ \varGamma_{F, \{\mathsf{com}_j\}_{j=1}^n} \left( m_{\mathsf{Ext}}, \mathsf{ST}_{C^*} \right) : \left( \{\mathsf{com}_j\}_{j=1}^n, m_{\mathsf{Ext}}, \mathsf{ST}_{C^*} \right) \leftarrow \mathcal{SE}_F^{C^*(\rho)}(1^\lambda, 1^{\varepsilon^{-1}}) \right\}_{\lambda} \\ &\overset{c}{\approx} \left\{ \varGamma_{F, \{\mathsf{com}_j\}_{j=1}^n} \left( F\left(\mathsf{val}_{\Pi}(\mathsf{com}_1), ..., \mathsf{val}_{\Pi}(\mathsf{com}_n) \right), \mathsf{ST}_{C^*} \right) \right\}_{\lambda} \\ &: \left( \{\mathsf{com}_j\}_{j=1}^n, \mathsf{ST}_{C^*}, \{b_{\mathsf{com},j}\}_{j=1}^n \right) \leftarrow \langle C^*(\rho), R^n \rangle (1^\lambda) \right\}_{\lambda} \end{split},$$

where  $(\{\mathsf{com}_j\}_{j=1}^n, \mathsf{ST}_{C^*}, \{b_{\mathsf{com},j}\}_{j=1}^n) \leftarrow \langle C^*(\rho), R^n \rangle (1^{\lambda})$  means that  $C^*(\rho)$  interacts with n copies of the honest receiver R in parallel and the execution results in transcripts  $\{\mathsf{com}_j\}_{j=1}^n$ , the final state  $\mathsf{ST}_{C^*}$ , and outputs  $\{b_{\mathsf{com},j}\}_{j=1}^n$  of each copy of R and

$$\Gamma_{F,\{\mathsf{com}_j\}_{j=1}^n}\left(m,\mathsf{ST}_{C^*}\right) \coloneqq \begin{cases} (m,\mathsf{ST}_{C^*}) & \textit{if } \{\mathsf{com}_j\}_{j=1}^n \textit{ is } F\textit{-good} \\ \bot & \textit{otherwise} \end{cases}.$$

<span id="page-32-1"></span>Lemma 14 (Special Parallel Weak Extractability with  $\varepsilon$ -Simulation). wExtCom satisfies the special parallel weak extractability with  $\varepsilon$ -simulation (as per Def. 16).

*Proof.* Since the proof is very similar to that of Lem. 13, we only highlight the differences from that. Similarly to the proof of Lem. 13, we assume that Com satisfies perfect binding for simplicity.

In a parallel interaction between a non-uniform QPT malicious committer  $C^*(\rho)$  and n copies of R, let  $\mathsf{wExtCom.com}_j$  be the commitment generated in the j-th session and let  $\overline{\mathsf{com}}_j = \{\mathsf{com}_{j,i}^0, \mathsf{com}_{j,i}^1\}_{i=1}^k$  be the part of  $\mathsf{wExtCom.com}_j$  that consists of the commitments of  $\mathsf{Com}$  generated in  $\mathsf{Step}\ 1$ . Remark

{33}------------------------------------------------

that the special parallel weak extractability with  $\varepsilon$ -simulation only requires the extractor to correctly extract and simulate if  $\{\text{wExtCom.com}_j\}_{j=1}^n$  is F-good. In this case,  $\{\overline{\text{com}}_j\}_{j=1}^n$  is also F-good in the following sense:

- there exists  $V \subseteq [n]$  such that  $\overline{\mathsf{com}}_j$  is valid (as per Def. 14) for all  $j \in V$ , and
- there exists  $m^*$  such that  $F(m'_1,...,m'_n)=m^*$  for all  $(m'_1,...,m'_n)$  such that  $m'_j=\mathsf{val}_{\mathsf{Com}}(\overline{\mathsf{com}}_j)$  for all  $j\in V$ .

Therefore, it suffices to prove that the extractor works for any fixed F-good  $\{\overline{\mathsf{com}}_j\}_{j=1}^n$ .

For  $\{\mathbf{c}_j\}_{j=1}^n \in (\{0,1\}^k)^n$ , let  $U_{\{\mathbf{c}_j\}_{j=1}^n}$  be the unitary corresponding to the action of  $C^*$  in Step 2 similarly to the proof of Lem. 13. Remark that the unitary is indexed by  $\{\mathbf{c}_j\}_{j=1}^n$  since we are considering an n-parallell execution. Similarly, we let  $\Pi_{\{\mathbf{c}_j\}_{j=1}^n}^{\mathsf{test}}$  be the projection that maps onto states that contain accepting openings of  $\overline{\mathsf{com}}_j$  w.r.t.  $\mathbf{c}_j$  for all  $j \in [n]$  in the designated registers. Then, we define  $\Pi_{\{\mathbf{c}_j\}_{j=1}^n} := U_{\{\mathbf{c}_j\}_{j=1}^n}^{\mathsf{test}} \Pi_{\{\mathbf{c}_j\}_{j=1}^n}^{\mathsf{test}} U_{\{\mathbf{c}_j\}_{j=1}^n}^n$ .

We apply Lem. 4 for  $\{\Pi_{\{\mathbf{c}_j\}_{i=1}^n}\}_{\{\mathbf{c}_j\}_{i=1}^n\in(\{0,1\}^k)^n}$  with the following correspondence.

- $-\mathcal{H}$  is the internal space of  $C^*$ .
- The initial state is  $\rho'$ .
- $C = (\{0,1\}^k)^n.$
- $-S = \{(\{c_{j,1}, ..., c_{j,k}\}\}_{j=1}^n, \{c'_{j,1}, ..., c'_{j,k}\}_{j=1}^n) : \forall j \in [n] \ \exists i \in [k] \ \text{s.t.} \ c_{j,i} \neq c'_{j,i}\}$
- $\mathcal{A}_0$  applies  $U_{\{\mathbf{c}_j\}_{j=1}^n}$  on its input, measures the designated registers to get the  $\mathbf{v}_j$  for each  $j \in [n]$ , applies  $U_{\{\mathbf{c}_j\}_{j=1}^n}^{\dagger}$ , and outputs  $\{\mathbf{v}_j\}_{j=1}^n$ .
- $\mathcal{A}_1$  is given as input  $(\{\mathbf{c}_j\}_{j=1}^n, \{\mathbf{c}_j'\}_{j=1}^n) \in S$ ,  $\mathbf{v}_{\{\mathbf{c}_j\}_{j=1}^n} = \{v_{j,1}^{c_{j,1}}, ..., v_{j,k}^{c_{j,k}}\}_{j=1}^n$ , and  $\mathbf{v}_{\{\mathbf{c}_j'\}_{j=1}^n} = \{v_{j,1}^{c_{j,1}}, ..., v_{j,k}^{c_{j,k}}\}_{j=1}^n$ .  $\mathcal{A}_1$  computes  $m_j := v_{j,i_j}^{c_{j,i_j}} \oplus v_{j,i_j}^{c_{j,i_j}}$  for  $j \in [n]$  where  $i_j \in [k]$  is the smallest index such that  $c_{j,i} \neq c_{j,i}'$  and outputs  $F(m_1, ..., m_n)$ . Note that such  $i_j$  exists for all  $j \in [n]$  since we assume  $(\{\mathbf{c}_j\}_{j=1}^n, \{\mathbf{c}_j'\}_{j=1}^n) \in S$ .

  If  $\{\overline{\mathsf{com}}_j\}_{j=1}^n$  is F-good, we can see that the assumptions for Lem. 4 are satisfied as follows.
- 1. By the definition of S and the union bound, it is easy to see that  $\frac{|S|}{|C|^2} \ge 1 n2^{-k} = 1 \text{negl}(\lambda)$ .
- 2. For any  $\{\mathbf{c}_j\}_{j=1}^n$ , if  $\mathcal{A}_0$  takes a state in the span of  $\Pi_{\{\mathbf{c}\}_{j=1}^n}$  as input, it outputs  $s_{\{\mathbf{c}_j\}_{j=1}^n} := \{\mathsf{val}_{\mathsf{Com}}(\mathsf{com}_{j,1}^{c_{j,1}}), ..., \mathsf{val}_{\mathsf{Com}}(\mathsf{com}_{j,k}^{c_{j,k}})\}_{j=1}^n$  with probability 1 by the definition of  $\Pi_{\{\mathbf{c}\}_{j=1}^n}$  and the perfect binding property of Com.
- 3. Let  $V \subseteq [n]$  be a subset for which the conditions of the F-goodness are satisfied. For any  $(\{\mathbf{c}_j\}_{j=1}^n, \{\mathbf{c}_j'\}_{j=1}^n) \in S$ , if  $A_1$  takes as input the  $s_{\{\mathbf{c}_j\}_{j=1}^n}$  and  $s_{\{\mathbf{c}_j'\}_{j=1}^n}$  defined as follows:

$$\begin{cases} s_{\{\mathbf{c}_j\}_{j=1}^n} := \{\mathsf{val}_{\mathsf{Com}}(\mathsf{com}_{j,1}^{c_{j,1}}), ..., \mathsf{val}_{\mathsf{Com}}(\mathsf{com}_{j,k}^{c_{j,k}})\}_{j=1}^n \\ s_{\{\mathbf{c}_j'\}_{j=1}^n} := \{\mathsf{val}_{\mathsf{Com}}(\mathsf{com}_{j,1}^{c_{j,1}}), ..., \mathsf{val}_{\mathsf{Com}}(\mathsf{com}_{j,k}^{c_{j,k}})\}_{j=1}^n \end{cases}$$

then, we have  $m_j = \mathsf{val}_{\mathsf{Com}}(\overline{\mathsf{com}}_j)$  for all  $j \in V$  since  $\overline{\mathsf{com}}_j$  is valid for all  $j \in V$ . Note that this may not hold for  $j \notin V$ . However, by the second condition of the F-goodness, there is  $m^*$  such that  $F(m_1, ..., m_n) = m^*$  regardless of the values of  $\{m_j\}_{j\notin V}$ . We can set  $s^* := m^*$ .

Then, the rest of the proof is identical to that of Lem. 13.

{34}------------------------------------------------

### <span id="page-34-9"></span><span id="page-34-0"></span>5.2 Strongly Extractable Commitment

In this section, we construct strongly extractable commitment with  $\varepsilon$ -simulation. The scheme is shown in Prot. 2. It relies on the following building blocks:

- 1. the  $\varepsilon$ -simulatable weakly extractable commitment wExtCom given in Prot. 1. We remark that the security of Prot. 2 relies on the particular wExtCom presented in Prot. 1 because we also need the special parallel weak extractability with  $\varepsilon$ -simulation (Def. 16); we do not know if Prot. 2 can be based on any wExtCom satisfying the weak extractability with  $\varepsilon$ -simulation as in Def. 13.
- 2. a (n+1,t)-perfectly verifiable secret sharing scheme  $VSS = (VSS_{Share}, VSS_{Recon})$  (as per Def. 1). We require that t is a constant fraction of n such that  $t \le n/3$ . There are known constructions (without any computational assumptions) satisfying these properties [BGW88, CDD<sup>+</sup>99].

#### <span id="page-34-1"></span>Protocol 2: $\varepsilon$ -Simulatable Strongly Extractable Commitment ExtCom

Let  $n(\lambda)$  be a polynomial on  $\lambda$ . Let t be a constant fraction of n such that  $t \leq n/3$ .

**Input:** both the (committer) C and the receiver R get security parameter  $1^{\lambda}$  as the common input; C gets a string  $m \in \{0,1\}^{\ell(\lambda)}$  as his private input, where  $\ell(\cdot)$  is a polynomial.

### Commit Stage:

- <span id="page-34-5"></span>1. C emulates n+1 (virtual) players  $\{P_i\}_{i\in[n+1]}$  to execute the  $VSS_{Share}$  protocol "in his head", where the input to  $P_{n+1}$  (i.e., the Dealer) is m. Let  $\{v_i\}_{i\in[n+1]}$  be the views of the n+1 players describing the execution.
- <span id="page-34-3"></span>2. C and R involve in n executions of wExtCom in parallel, where in the i-th instance  $(i \in [n])$ , C commits to  $v_i$ .
- <span id="page-34-2"></span>3. R picks a random string  $r_1$  and commits to it using wExtCom.
- <span id="page-34-8"></span>4. C picks a random string  $r_2$  and sends it to R.
- <span id="page-34-7"></span>5. R sends to C the value  $r_1$  together with the corresponding decommitment information w.r.t. the wExtCom in Step 3. Now, both parties learn a coin-tossing result  $r = r_1 \oplus r_2$ , which specifies a size-t random subset  $T \subseteq [n]$ .
- <span id="page-34-4"></span>6. C sends to R in one round the following messages:  $\{v_i\}_{i\in T}$  together with the corresponding decommitment information w.r.t. the wExtCom in Step 2.
- 7. R checks the following conditions:
  - (a) All the decommitments in Step 6 are valid; and
  - (b) for any  $i, j \in T$ , views  $(v_i, v_j)$  are consistent (as per Def. 3 and Rmk. 2) w.r.t. the VSS<sub>Share</sub> execution as described in Step 1.

If all the checks pass, R accepts; otherwise, R rejects.

#### Decommit Stage:

- <span id="page-34-6"></span>1. C sends  $\{v_i\}_{i\in[n]}$  together with all the corresponding information w.r.t. the wExtCom in Step 1 of the Commit Stage.
- 2. R constructs  $\{v_i'\}_{i\in[n]}$  as follows: in Step 1 of the Decommit Stage, if the i-th decommitment is valid, R sets  $v_i' := v_i$ ; otherwise, R sets  $v_i' := \bot$ .
- 3. R outputs  $m' := VSS_{Recon}(v'_1, \dots, v'_n)$ .

**Proof of Security.** Correctness and statistically-binding property of ExtCom follows straightforwardly from that of wExtCom. In the following, we prove that ExtCom is computationally-hiding and (parallel) strong extractable with  $\varepsilon$ -simulation.

**Lemma 15** (Computational Hiding). ExtCom is computationally hiding.

{35}------------------------------------------------

*Proof.* We consider an interaction between the honest sender C and a malicious non-uniform QPT receiver  $R^*(\rho)$ . We consider the following two cases:

Case 1: If C rejects the decommitment in Step 5, any commitment of wExtCom generated in Step 2 is not decomitted. In this case, it is straightforward to reduce the computational hiding to that of wExtCom.

Case 2: If C accepts the decommitment in Step 5, the commitment of wExtCom generated in Step 3 is valid (as per Def. 10). We consider the following hybrid experiments, where  $H_0$  denotes the real execution  $\langle C, R^*(\rho) \rangle (1^{\lambda})$ :

- Hybrid  $H_1$ : This hybrid works similarly to  $H_0$  except that we run the  $\varepsilon$ -simulation weak extractor to extract  $r_1$  from the commitment in Step 3. Since the commitment is valid in this case as observed above, the final output of  $R^*$  is  $\varepsilon$ -close to that in  $H_0$  for arbitrarily small noticeable  $\varepsilon$ .
- Hybrid  $H_2$ : In the next hybrid, we first randomly pick a size-t random subset T at the beginning, and define  $r_2$  so that  $r = r_1 \oplus r_2$  specifies T in Step 4. Since  $r_2$  is uniformly distributed in either case, this is perfectly indistinguishable from  $H_1$ .
- Hybrid  $H_3$ : This hybrid is identical to  $H_2$ , except that in Step 1, for all  $i \in [n] \setminus T$ , we set  $v_i$  to an all-0 string of proper length.
  - $\underline{H_2} \stackrel{c}{\approx} \underline{H_3}$ : We now reduce the indistinguishability between  $H_2$  and  $H_3$  to the computationally-hiding property of (parallel executions of)<sup>30</sup> wExtCom. Consider an adversary  $\mathcal{A}$  participating in the hiding game of wExtCom.  $\mathcal{A}$  finishes Step 1 as in  $H_2$ , where the views of the n parties are denoted as  $\{v_i\}_{i\in[n]}$ . It also prepare (n-t) "null views"  $\{v_i'\}_{i\in[n]\setminus T}$  where each of them is an all-0 string of proper length.  $\mathcal{A}$  sends  $\{v_i\}_{i\in[n]\setminus T}$  and  $\{v_i'\}_{i\in[n]\setminus T}$  to the external challenger as his challenges for the parallel hiding game of wExtCom. Then,  $\mathcal{A}$  generates the Step 2 commitment (in parallel) in the following manner:
  - For  $i \in T$ : A commits to  $\{v_i\}_{i \in T}$  to (the internal)  $R^*$  using wExtCom himself;
  - For  $i \in [n] \setminus T$ : A relays the external challenger's wExtCom commitments to the internal  $R^*$ .

Finally,  $\mathcal{A}$  finishes the remaining steps of the Commit Stage as in  $H_3$  (or equivalently,  $H_2$ ). We remark that in Step 6,  $\mathcal{A}$  needs to decommit to the views corresponding to T, which he can because these commitments are generated by  $\mathcal{A}$  himself.

- Now, observe that if the external challenger commits to  $\{v_i\}_{i\in[n]\setminus T}$ , then  $\mathcal{A}$  is identical to  $H_2$ ; if the external challenger commits to  $\{v_i'\}_{i\in[n]\setminus T}$ , then  $\mathcal{A}$  is identical to  $H_3$ . Therefore,  $H_2 \stackrel{c}{\approx} H_3$  as otherwise  $\mathcal{A}$  wins the parallel hiding game.
- Hybrid  $H_4$ : This hybrid is identical to  $H_3$ , except that in Step 1, we run the simulator guaranteed by the Secrecy property (Property 2) of VSS to fake the views of parties in set  $T: \{v'_i\}_{i \in [T]} \leftarrow \mathcal{S}^{\mathcal{A}}(1^{\lambda}, T)$ , and for each  $j \in [n] \setminus T$ , we set  $v'_j$  to all-0 strings of proper length. Note that this hybrid does not need to know m anymore. It is easy to see that  $H_3 \stackrel{\text{i.d.}}{\Longrightarrow} H_4$  due to the perfect secrecy of VSS.

The above argument implies that for any  $m_0$  and  $m_1$ , the outputs of  $R^*$  when the message is  $m_0$  or  $m_1$  are indistinguishable with advantage at most  $2\varepsilon$ . Since  $\varepsilon$  is arbitrarily small noticeable function, the above implies that they are computationally indistinguishable in the standard sense.

Since we know that one of Case 1 and Case 2 must happen, the overall reduction algorithm can first guess which case occurs and run the reduction algorithm corresponding to the guessed case; when the guess turns out to be incorrect, it simply outputs a uniform bit. Since the guess is correct with probability 1/2, this reduction works with a security loss of the multiplicative factor 1/2.<sup>31</sup>

<span id="page-35-0"></span><sup>&</sup>lt;sup>30</sup> Note that the computationally-hiding property of parallel executions follows from that of the stand-alone execution by a standard hybrid argument.

<span id="page-35-1"></span><sup>&</sup>lt;sup>31</sup> If we use Watrous' rewinding lemma (Lem. 2), we may avoid the security loss; but this is not needed here.

{36}------------------------------------------------

In the following, we prove the (parallel-)strong extractability with ε-simulation. Though we finally prove the parallel version, we first give a proof for the stand-alone version since that is simpler and the proof is readily extended to that of the parallel version.

<span id="page-36-3"></span>Lemma 16 (Strong Extractability with ε-Simulation). ExtCom is strongly extractable with εsimulation (as per [Def. 11\)](#page-20-2).

Proof. Suppose that a non-uniform QPT committer C ∗ interacts with the honest receiver R in the commit stage of ExtCom. We consider two cases where R accepts or rejects, respectively. By using Watrous' rewinding lemma [\(Lem. 2\)](#page-16-3) in a similar way to the proof of [Lem. 4,](#page-21-2) it suffices to construct a simulator that correctly extracts and simulates for each case separately. Moreover, when R rejects, the commitment is invalid and thus the extractor does not need to extract anything. Thus, there is a trivial perfect simulation extractor for this case: it can simply run the interaction between C ∗ (ρ) and R by playing the role of R and outputs the final state of C ∗ . What is left is to construct an extractor that correctly extracts and simulates assuming that R accepts in the committing stage. That is, it suffices to prove the following claim.

<span id="page-36-0"></span>Claim 6 (Extraction and Simulation for Accepting Case). There exists a QPT algorithm SEAcc such that for any noticeable ε(λ) and any non-uniform QPT C ∗ (ρ), it holds that

$$\begin{split} & \left\{ \varGamma_{b_{\text{com}}}(m_{\text{Ext}}, \text{ST}_{C^*}) : (m_{\text{Ext}}, \text{ST}_{C^*}, b_{\text{com}}) \leftarrow \mathcal{SE}_{\text{Acc}}^{C^*(\rho)}(1^{\lambda}, 1^{\varepsilon^{-1}}) \right\}_{\lambda} \\ & \stackrel{c}{\approx}_{\varepsilon} \left\{ \varGamma_{b_{\text{com}}}(\text{val}_{\text{ExtCom}}(\text{com}), \text{ST}_{C^*}) : (\text{com}, \text{ST}_{C^*}, b_{\text{com}}) \leftarrow \langle C^*(\rho), R \rangle (1^{\lambda}) \right\}_{\lambda}, \end{split}$$

.

where 
$$\Gamma_{b_{\text{com}}}(m, \mathsf{ST}_{C^*}) \coloneqq \begin{cases} (m, \mathsf{ST}_{C^*}) & \text{if } b_{\text{com}} = 1 \\ \bot & \text{otherwise} \end{cases}$$

Remark 5. One may think that the above claim is similar to the weak extractability with ε-simulation [\(Def. 13\)](#page-30-0). However, the crucial difference is that the extractor SEAcc should declare if the simulation has succeeded by outputting bcom in the clear. On the other hand, in [Def. 13,](#page-30-0) SEweak is only required to indirectly declare that depending on if com is valid, which may not be known by SEweak.

Proof of [Claim 6.](#page-36-0) Let wExtCom.com<sup>i</sup> be the i-th commitment of wExtCom in [Step 2](#page-34-3) in the commit stage. In the execution of (com, STC<sup>∗</sup> , bcom) ← hC ∗ (ρ), Ri(1<sup>λ</sup> ), let Good be the event that {wExtCom.comi} n i=1 is VSSRecon-good in the sense of [Def. 16,](#page-32-0) i.e.,

- there exists V ⊆ [n] such that wExtCom.com<sup>i</sup> is valid (i.e., valwExtCom(wExtCom.comi) 6= ⊥) for all i ∈ V , and
- there exists m<sup>∗</sup> such that VSSRecon(v 0 1 , . . . , v 0 n ) = m<sup>∗</sup> for all (v 0 1 , . . . , v 0 n ) such that

$$\forall i \in V, \ \mathsf{v}_i' = \mathsf{val}_{\mathsf{wExtCom}}(\mathsf{wExtCom}.\mathsf{com}_i).$$

Let Bad be the complementary event of Good. We prove the following claim.

<span id="page-36-1"></span>Claim 7. It holds that

<span id="page-36-2"></span>
$$\Pr\left[\mathsf{Bad} \wedge b_{\mathsf{com}} = 1 : (\mathsf{com}, \mathsf{ST}_{C^*}, b_{\mathsf{com}}) \leftarrow \langle C^*(\rho), R \rangle (1^{\lambda})\right] = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda). \tag{7}$$

Assuming [Claim 7,](#page-36-1) it is straightforward to finish the proof of [Claim 6](#page-36-0) by using [Lem. 14.](#page-32-1) [Claim 7](#page-36-1) means that the Good occurs whenever bcom = 1 except for negligible probability. Since SEAcc is only required to correctly extract and simulate when bcom = 1, it suffices to give an extractor that correctly 

{37}------------------------------------------------

<span id="page-37-1"></span>extracts and simulates when  $\{w\text{ExtCom.com}_i\}_{i=1}^n$  satisfies the condition for Good. Since wExtCom satisfies the special parallel weak extractability with  $\varepsilon$ -simulation as shown in Lem. 14,  $\mathcal{SE}_{VSS_{Recon}}$  given in Def. 16 (where we set  $F := VSS_{Recon}$ ) directly gives  $\mathcal{SE}_{Acc}$ . Specifically,  $\mathcal{SE}_{Acc}$  as described below suffices for Claim 6.

$$\mathcal{SE}^{C^*(\rho)}_{\mathsf{Acc}}(1^{\lambda},1^{\varepsilon^{-1}})$$
:

- 1. Run  $(\{\mathsf{wExtCom.com}_i\}_{i=1}^n, m_{\mathsf{Ext}}, \mathsf{ST}_{C_2^*}) \leftarrow \mathcal{SE}_{\mathsf{VSS}_{\mathsf{Recon}}}^{C_2^*(\rho)}(1^{\lambda}, 1^{\varepsilon^{-1}}) \text{ where } C_2^* \text{ denotes the action of } C^* \text{ until Step 2} \text{ in the commit stage where it outputs } \{\mathsf{wExtCom.com}_i\}_{i=1}^n.$
- 2. Simulate the interaction between  $C^*$  and R from Step 3 where the state of  $C^*$  is initialized to be  $\mathsf{ST}_{C_2^*}$ . Let  $b_{\mathrm{com}}$  be R's decision (i.e.,  $b_{\mathrm{com}} = 1$  if and only if R accepts) and  $\mathsf{ST}_{C^*}$  be the post-execution state of S
- 3. Output  $(m_{\mathsf{Ext}}, \mathsf{ST}_{C^*}, b_{\mathsf{com}})$ .

Now, the only thing left is to prove Claim 7.

*Proof of Claim* 7. This proof follows from a similar argument which has been used to establish the soundness of the [IKOS07] commit-and-prove protocol [IKOS07, GLOV12, GOSV14, LP21].

Let  $\mathsf{v}_i^* \coloneqq \mathsf{val}_{\mathsf{wExtCom}}(\mathsf{wExtCom}.\mathsf{com}_i)$  for all  $i \in [n]$ . We now define an object called *inconsistency* graph. This is an undirected graph G with n vertices, where the i-th vertex corresponds to  $\mathsf{v}_i^*$ ; there is an edge between vertices i and j in G if only only if  $\mathsf{v}_i^*$  and  $\mathsf{v}_j^*$  are inconsistent (as per Def. 3) w.r.t. the  $\mathsf{VSS}_{\mathsf{Share}}$  execution. Let  $B_G$  denote that set of vertices that form a minimum vertex cover<sup>32</sup> of G (When G is not unique, pick one arbitrarily). Next, we prove Eq. (7) by considering the following two possibilities based on the size of G:

If  $|B_G| \le t$ : we argue that except with negligible probability, either the even Good will happen, or we must have  $b_{\text{com}} = 0$ .

To see that, consider an execution of VSS<sub>Share</sub> where an adversary corrupts the set of players in  $B_G$ , and behaves in a way that the views of any player  $P_j$ , for  $j \notin B_G$ , is  $\mathsf{v}_j^*$ . Such an execution can be obtained by choosing all the messages from  $P_j \in B_G$  to  $P_j \notin B_G$  as in the view  $\mathsf{v}_j^*$ ; since  $B_G$  is a vertex cover, every pair of views  $(\mathsf{v}_i^*, \mathsf{v}_j^*)$  with  $i, j \in \overline{B_G}$  are not connected in the graph G and hence consistent. Finally, by the (n+1,t)-perfect verifiable-committing property of VSS (see Property 1), such a corruption should not influence the output of the honest players in the VSS<sub>Share</sub> stage. That is, one of the following two cases (corresponding to the two possibilities listed in Property 1) must happen:

- 1. During the Sharing Phase, all honest players (i.e., those in  $\overline{B_G}$ ) disqualify the dealer. In this case, for each  $i \in \overline{B_G}$ ,  $\mathsf{v}_i^*$  contains a special symbol  $\bot$  indicating the failure of the Sharing Phase. Recall that R checks a size-t random subset (determined by the coin-flipping in Steps 3 to 5) of  $\{v_i^*\}_{i \in [n]}$ . Since  $|\overline{B_G}| > n t$  and t is a constant fraction of n, R will pick at least one  $v^*i$  for  $i \in \overline{B_G}$  with overwhelming probability; in this case, R learns the failure of the Sharing Phase and thus rejects the Commit Phase (i.e.,  $b_{\text{com}} = 0$ ).
- 2. During the Sharing Phase, honest players do not disqualify the dealer. Therefore such a phase determines a unique value  $m^*$  such that  $VSS_{Recon}(v_1^*, \ldots, v_n^*) = m^*$ , which implies that  $\{wExtCom.com_i\}_{i=1}^n$  is  $VSS_{Recon}$ -good in the sense of Def. 16 (with  $\overline{B_G}$  playing the role of the set V in Def. 16). Put in other words, the even Good is happening now.

Remark 6. Notice that in the above argument, it is essential that the verifiable-committing property of VSS is perfect (see Def. 1), because it implies that that following types of corruption do not hurt

<span id="page-37-0"></span><sup>&</sup>lt;sup>32</sup> Recall that a *vertex cover* of a graph is a set of vertices that includes at least one endpoint of every edge of the graph.

{38}------------------------------------------------

<span id="page-38-2"></span>the security of VSS: (1) semi-honest corruption; (2) semi-honest corruption with maliciously chosen randomness. Therefore, an "effective corruption" must create inconsistency with at least one honest party. Indeed, if this property is only statistical, extra efforts are needed to finish this proof. See [IKOS07] for details.

If  $|B_G| > t$ : we argue that  $b_{\text{com}} = 1$  (i.e., R accepts at the end of the Commit Stage) with at most negligible probability. Recall that R checks the consistency of a size-t random subset of all the views  $\{v_i^*\}_{i \in [n]}$  (i.e., vertices in G). We only need to argue that such a checking will hit an edge in G with overwhelming probability. For this, we use the well-known connection between the size of a minimum vertex cover to the size of a maximum matching. Concretely, the graph G must have a matching<sup>33</sup>  $\mathcal{M}$  of size at least t/2. (Otherwise, if the maximum matching contains less than t/2 edges, then the vertices of this matching form a vertex cover set B with |B| < t.) Recall that if R hits any edge of G, he will reject. The probability that the t vertices (views) that R picks miss all the edges of G is smaller than the probability that he misses all edges of the matching, which is again at most  $2^{-\Omega(t)} = 2^{-\Omega(\lambda)}$ . To see that, suppose that the first t/2 vertices picked by R do not hit an edge of the matching. Denote this set of vertices as  $S_{t/2}$ .
