That is, for any noticeable function ε(λ), the simulator will generate a view for the corrupted party that is at most ε-far from that party's view in the real-world execution; the running time of the simulator should be a polynomial on both the security parameter and 1/ε [\(Def. 25\)](#page-50-2). We remark that ε-simulatable security is effectively the best one can hope for, because constant-round post-quantum 2PC satisfying the standard negl(λ) close simulation cannot exist, unless either non-black-box simulation is used to prove security or NP ⊆ BQP [\[CCLY21\]](#page-58-8).

To deal with these issues, we first note that the classical recipe from [Sec. 7.3](#page-52-0) actually extends to the post-quantum setting (i.e., when the adversaries are QPT), because the simulators in both [Lem. 23](#page-54-5) and [24](#page-54-4) are straight-line and non-cloning (this has also been observed in previous works, e.g., [\[Unr10,](#page-61-13) [ABG](#page-58-20)+21b]); and as mentioned earlier, [Lem. 25](#page-55-2) was originally proven in the postquantum setting directly. Therefore, it seems that we immediately obtain a post-quantum version of [Thm. 11.](#page-55-3) But there is one more caveat[—Thm. 11](#page-55-3) also relies on the aforementioned non-concurrent composition lemma. In particular, the F t ot functionality assumed in [Lem. 24](#page-54-4) is realized by the realworld protocol (albeit in the F t so-com-hybrid model) induced by [Lem. 23](#page-54-5) (together with [Lem. 25\)](#page-55-2). Therefore, we need to make sure that such a non-concurrent composition is also applicable in the post-quantum setting. Fortunately, this follows from [Lem. 22](#page-51-0) which we proved in [Sec. 7.2.](#page-50-0) The above discussion leads to the following post-quantum and ε-simulatable version of [Thm. 11:](#page-55-3)

Theorem 13. There is a polynomial t(λ) such that there exists a black-box construction of ε-simulatable 2PC protocol secure against static, malicious QPT adversaries in the stand-alone F t so-com-hybrid model, starting from any bit-OT protocol secure against static, semi-honest QPT adversaries. Moreover, the construction achieves a constant multiplicative blow up in the number of rounds.

Now, to finish the proof of [Thm. 12,](#page-55-4) we only need to show the following [Lem. 26,](#page-56-0) and then invoke the non-concurrent composition lemma [\(Lem. 22\)](#page-51-0) again, to replace the F t so-com in [Thm. 13](#page-56-1) with the semi-honest OT based construction from [Lem. 26.](#page-56-0)

Lemma 26. For any polynomial t(λ), there exists a constant-round protocol that implements F t so-com w.r.t. ε-simulatable security against QPT adversaries. This construction makes only black-box access to any OWFs secure against QPT adversaries[40](#page-56-2) .

Proof. We show a ε-simulatable protocol implementing F t so-com, assuming only black-box access to OWFs secure against QPT adversaries. We will again rely on the black-box ExtCom-and-Prove as per [Def. 17.](#page-39-2) The committer will commit to the t messages (m1, . . . , mt) using the Commit Stage of the ExtCom-and-Prove. To decommit to the messages determined by the receiver's choice of I ⊆ [n], the committer first sends {mi}i∈<sup>I</sup> to the receiver; then, both parties execute the Prove Stage, where the committer proves the following predicate:

<span id="page-56-1"></span><span id="page-56-0"></span>
$$\phi_{I,\{m_i\}_{i\in I}}(x) = \begin{cases} 1 & \text{if } (x = m_1' \| \dots \| m_t') \land (\forall i \in I, m_i' = m_i) \\ 0 & \text{otherwise} \end{cases} . \tag{16}$$

<span id="page-56-2"></span><sup>40</sup> Note that such OWFs can be constructed from any post-quantum semi-honest OT in black-box.

{57}------------------------------------------------

<span id="page-57-6"></span>That is,  $\phi_{I,\{m_i\}_{i\in I}}(\cdot)$  has I and |I|-many messages hard-wired; it evaluates to 1 if and only if its input can be parsed as  $(m'_1||\ldots,||m'_t)$  of the correct length, and  $m'_i$  agrees with  $m_i$  for all i's specified by I.

This construction is constant-round and based on black-box access to post-quantum secure OWFs, because the ExtCom-and-Prove scheme is so. To prove security, if the committer is corrupted, simulation can be done via the  $\varepsilon$ -simulatable extractability of the Commit Stage (Property 1); if the receiver is corrupted, simulation can be done via the  $\varepsilon$ -ZK property (Property 4) of the ExtCom-and-Prove.

## <span id="page-57-0"></span>8 Black-Box Constant-Round $\varepsilon$ -2PC using Quantum Communication

If quantum communication is allowed, we can obtain a constant-round  $\varepsilon$ -simulatable PQ-2PC assuming only black-box access to post-quantum secure OWFs (instead of post-quantum secure semi-honest OTs). Recently, [GLSV21] and [BCKM21] (based on earlier works [CK90, BBCS92, DFL<sup>+</sup>09, BF10]) constructed fully simulatable (in contrast to  $\varepsilon$ -simulatable) post-quantum 2PC (actually, they obtained MPC) assuming only post-quantum secure OWFs and quantum communication. The protocol from [BCKM21] makes only black-box use of the assumed OWF. But neither of these constructions is in constant rounds (without trusted assumptions like common reference strings).

Our construction follows the same path shown in Sec. 7.4. That is, we break the task of constructing  $\varepsilon$ -simulatable PQ-2PC in to the following steps:

- <span id="page-57-3"></span>1. Construct a constant-round  $\varepsilon$ -simulatable protocol implementing  $\mathcal{F}_{\text{SO-COM}}^{t'}$ ;
- <span id="page-57-2"></span>2. In the  $\mathcal{F}_{\text{SO-COM}}^{t'}$ -hybrid model, construct a constant-round  $\varepsilon$ -simulatable protocol implementing  $\mathcal{F}_{\text{OT}}^{t}$ ;
- <span id="page-57-4"></span>3. In the  $\mathcal{F}_{\text{OT}}^t$ -hybrid model, construct a constant-round  $\varepsilon$ -simulatable PQ-2PC protocol.

Notice that in Sec. 7 where only classical communication is allowed, the only place where the post-quantum semi-honest OT is used is Step 2. (Steps 1 and 3 only need to make black-box access to a post-quantum secure OWF.) Therefore, we will obtain the desired construction if we can make Step 2 work without relying on post-quantum secure semi-honest OTs.

<span id="page-57-5"></span>We observe that [GLSV21] already did this relying on the [BBCS92] OT construction, which makes use of quantum communication. We refer the read to [GLSV21] for further details. Here, we simply import the related lemma from [GLSV21].

Lemma 27 ([GLSV21, Theorem 3.2, Corollary 3.4]). For any polynomial  $t(\lambda)$ , there exists another polynomial  $t'(\lambda)$  such that there is a constant-round protocol implementing  $\mathcal{F}_{\text{OT}}^t$  w.r.t.  $\varepsilon$ -simulatable security against QPT adversaries in the  $\mathcal{F}_{\text{SO-COM}}^{t'}$ -hybrid model. This protocol makes use of quantum communication.

Replacing Step 2 with the construction promised by Lem. 27 yields our final theorem. (Recall that we already finished Steps 1 and 3 in Sec. 7.)

**Theorem 14.** Assuming the existence of OWFs secure against QPT adversaries, there exists a black-box, constant-round construction of  $\varepsilon$ -simulatable 2PC protocol secure against QPT adversaries. This protocol makes use of quantum communication.

## <span id="page-62-3"></span>Appendix

## <span id="page-62-0"></span>A From Extractable Commitment to ZK Argument

We give a proof sketch for that constant-round post-quantum extractable commitment with blackbox extraction implies constant-round post-quautum ZK argument for NP. The construction also relies on Σ-protocol (say, Blum's Hamiltonicity protocol [\[Blu86\]](#page-58-21)). Then the ZK argument works as follows:

- 1. The prover sends the first message of the Σ-protocol.
- 2. The prover and verifier jointly run one-sided simulation coin-flipping based on the extractable commitment to determine the second message of Σ-protocol. That is, they do the following:
  - (a) The verifier commits to a uniformly random string r<sup>1</sup> of the same length as the second message of Σ-protocol by using the extractable commitment.
  - (b) The prover sends a uniformly random string r<sup>2</sup> of the same length in the clear.
  - (c) The verifier opens r1, and the second message of the Σ-protocol is set to be r<sup>1</sup> ⊕ r2.
- 3. The prover sends the third message of the Σ-protocol.
- 4. The verifier runs the verification algorithm of the Σ-protocol.

It is clear that the above protocol is constant-round if the extractable commitment is constantround. For soundness, we show that no QPT cheating prover can bias r<sup>1</sup> ⊕ r<sup>2</sup> based on the computational hiding of the extractable commitment. Then, the soundness of the above protocol can be easily reduced to that of the underlying Σ-protocol. For ZK, recall that Σ-protocol satisfies a special honest-verifier ZK, which enables one to simulate the transcript if the second message is known in advance. Based on that, we construct a simulator for the above protocol as follows. It first randomly chooses the second message β of the Σ-protocol. Then it simulates the transcript (α, β, γ) of the Σ-protocol and sends α to the malicious verifier as the first message. Then it extracts r<sup>1</sup> from the malicious verifier while simulating its state by running the extractor and sets r<sup>2</sup> := r<sup>1</sup> ⊕ β and sends r<sup>2</sup> to the malicious verifier. Finally, it sends γ to the malicious verifier as the final message. It is straightforward to show that this simulator satisfies the requirement for ZK. Moreover, this simulator is black-box as long as the extractor is black-box.

## <span id="page-62-1"></span>B Postponed Proofs in [Sec. 4](#page-21-0)

### <span id="page-62-2"></span>B.1 Proof of [Lem. 10](#page-24-1)

Proof of [Lem. 10.](#page-24-1) Since the proof is very similar to that of [\[CCY21,](#page-58-7) Lemma 3.3], we only explain the differences. We define projections Π<sup>0</sup> and Π<sup>1</sup> over H = H<sup>X</sup> × H<sup>Y</sup> as

$$\Pi_0 := I_{\mathbf{X}} \otimes (|0\rangle \langle 0|)_{\mathbf{Y}}, \Pi_1 := \Pi,$$

and apply Jordan's lemma similarly to in the proof of [\[CCY21,](#page-58-7) Lemma 3.2]. That is, S≥<sup>δ</sup> (resp. S<δ) is defined to be the subspace spanned by eigenvectors of Π0Π1Π<sup>0</sup> with eigenvalues ≥ δ (resp. < δ). Then [Items 1](#page-24-5) and [2](#page-24-4) directly follow from Jordan's lemma. For proving [Items 3](#page-25-7) and [4,](#page-25-4) we slightly modify the definitions of <sup>U</sup>amp,T and Amp from those in [\[CCY21,](#page-58-7) Lemma 3.2]. We first consider an algorithm Amp <sup>g</sup> described as follows:

{63}------------------------------------------------

<span id="page-63-0"></span> $\widetilde{\mathsf{Amp}}(1^T, |\psi\rangle_{\mathbf{X}, \mathbf{Y}})$ : This algorithm takes a repetition parameter T and a quantum state  $|\psi\rangle_{\mathbf{X}, \mathbf{Y}} \in \mathcal{H}$  as input and works as follows:<sup>41</sup>

- 1. Repeat the following for i = 1, ..., T:
  - (a) Perform a measurement  $\{\Pi_0, I_{\mathbf{X},\mathbf{Y}} \Pi_0\}$ . If the outcome is 1, i.e., if  $\Pi_0$  is applied, then set  $A_i := 1$ .
  - (b) Perform a measurement  $\{\Pi_1, I_{\mathbf{X},\mathbf{Y}} \Pi_1\}$  If the outcome is 1, i.e., if  $\Pi_1$  is applied, then set  $B_i := 1$ .
  - (c) If  $A_i = B_i = 1$ , output the state in the registers  $(\mathbf{X}, \mathbf{Y})$  and a classical bit b = 1 indicating a success and immediately halt.
- 2. Output the state in the registers  $(\mathbf{X}, \mathbf{Y})$  and a classical bit b = 0 indicating a failure.

Remark 10. In the proof of [CCY21, Lemma 3.2], Amp outputs the state of  $(\mathbf{X}, \mathbf{Y})$  as soon as observing  $B_i = 1$  for some i, but here, it outputs the state only after observing  $A_i = 1$  and  $B_i = 1$  for some i. This modification is needed to ensure that the output state is in the span of  $\Pi_1 \Pi_0$  (rather than in the span of  $\Pi_1$  as in [CCY21, Lemma 3.2]).

We define an algorithm Amp as a purified version of Amp. That is, Amp works similarly to Amp except that intermediate measurement results are stored in designated registers in Anc without being measured and the output b is stored in register B. Let  $U_{\mathsf{amp},T}$  be the unitary part of  $\mathsf{Amp}(1^T,\cdot)$ . Then Item 3a and item 4 follows from the definition and Item 3c follows from the fact that  $S_{\geq \delta}$  and  $S_{<\delta}$  are invariant under  $\Pi_0$  and  $\Pi_1$ . For proving Item 3b, it suffices to consider the case where  $|\phi\rangle_{\mathbf{X}}|0\rangle_{\mathbf{Y}}$  is an eigenvector of  $\Pi_0\Pi_1\Pi_0$  with an eigenvalue  $t \geq \delta$  by Jordan's lemma as argued in the proof of [CCY21, Lemma 3.2]. In this case,  $\{A_i\}$  and  $\{B_i\}$  follow the following distribution:

- $\Pr[A_1 = 1] = 1$  and for all  $i \ge 2$ , the distribution of  $A_i$  only depends on  $B_{i-1}$  and  $\Pr[A_i = B_{i-1}] = t$ .
- For  $i \geq 1$ , the distribution of  $B_i$  only depends on  $A_i$  and  $\Pr[B_i = A_i] = t$ .

It suffices to show that for any noticeable  $t = t(\lambda)$  and  $\nu = \nu(\lambda)$ , there is  $T = \mathsf{poly}(\lambda)$  such that

$$\Pr[\exists i \in [T] \ A_i = B_i = 1] \ge 1 - \nu.$$
 (17)

First, note that

<span id="page-63-2"></span>
$$\Pr[A_1 = B_1 = 1] = t.$$

Thus, if  $t \ge 1 - \nu$ , then Eq. (17) is satisfied for T = 1. Below, we consider the case where  $t < 1 - \nu$ . For any  $i \ge 1$ , we have

$$\Pr[A_{i+1} = B_{i+1} = 1 \mid B_i = 0] = t(1-t)$$

and

$$\Pr[A_{i+1} = B_{i+1} = 1 \mid B_i = 1] = t^2.$$

Thus, for any positive integer T, we have

$$\Pr[\exists i \in [T] \ A_i = B_i = 1] \ge t + (1 - t)(1 - (1 - \min\{t(1 - t), t^2\})^{T - 1}).$$

Since  $\min\{t(1-t), t^2\}$  is noticeable, we can take  $T = \mathsf{poly}(\lambda)$  in such a way that  $t + (1-t)(1-(1-\min\{t(1-t), t^2\})^{T-1}) \ge 1 - \nu$ . This completes the proof.

<span id="page-63-1"></span>Strictly speaking, we need to consider descriptions of quantum circuits to perform measurements  $\{\Pi_0, I_{\mathbf{X},\mathbf{Y}} - \Pi_0\}$  and  $\{\Pi_1, I_{\mathbf{X},\mathbf{Y}} - \Pi_1\}$  as part of its input so that we can make the description of  $\widetilde{\mathsf{Amp}}$  independent on them. (Looking ahead, this is needed for showing Item 4 in Lemma 10 where  $\mathsf{Amp}$  is required to be a uniform QPT machine.) We omit to explicitly write them in the input of  $\widetilde{\mathsf{Amp}}$  for notational simplicity.

{64}------------------------------------------------

#### <span id="page-64-0"></span>B.2 Proof of Lem. 8

*Proof of Lem. 8.* If  $(12\gamma^{1/2} + 2\delta)^{1/2} > 1$ , then the desired inequality trivially holds. Thus, we assume  $(12\gamma^{1/2} + 2\delta)^{1/2} \le 1$  in the rest of the proof.

F can be represented by a projector  $\Pi$  over the input register and auxiliary input register with which F works as follows. F appends the ancillary register initialized to  $|0^n\rangle$ , performs the measurement  $\{\Pi, I - \Pi\}$  on the input and ancillary registers, and outputs the state in a designated output register tracing out all other registers if the state is projected onto  $\Pi$ , and otherwise outputs Fail.

We consider an additional one-qubit register and define

$$|\psi_b\rangle := \sqrt{1 - p_b} |0\rangle |0^m\rangle |0^n\rangle + |1\rangle \Pi |\phi_b\rangle |0^n\rangle$$

for  $b \in \{0,1\}$  where m is the number of qubits in the register for  $|\phi_b\rangle$  and

$$p_b := ||\Pi| |\phi_b\rangle |0^n\rangle ||^2.$$

Without loss of generality, we assume  $p_0 \ge p_1$ . It suffices to prove

$$\| |\psi_0\rangle \langle \psi_0| - |\psi_1\rangle \langle \psi_1| \|_{tr} \le (12\gamma^{1/2} + 2\delta)^{1/2}$$
 (18)

because a distinguisher that distinguishes  $F(|\phi_0\rangle\langle\phi_0|)$  and  $F(|\phi_1\rangle\langle\phi_1|)$  can be easily converted into a distinguisher that distinguishes  $|\psi_0\rangle$  and  $|\psi_1\rangle$  with the same advantage.

We show the following claim.

<span id="page-64-1"></span>Claim 15. The following holds:

<span id="page-64-2"></span>1. 
$$\|\Pi |\phi_{b,0}\rangle |0^n\rangle \|^2 \leq \gamma \text{ for } b \in \{0,1\}.$$

<span id="page-64-3"></span>2. 
$$\|\Pi |\phi_{b,1}\rangle |0^n\rangle \|^2 \ge p_b - 3\gamma^{1/2} \text{ for } b \in \{0,1\}.$$

Proof of Claim 15. Item 1 follows from the definition of  $\Pi$  and the assumption that  $\Pr\left[F\left(\frac{|\phi_{b,0}\rangle\langle\phi_{b,0}|}{\||\phi_{b,0}\rangle\|^2}\right) = \mathsf{Fail}\right] \ge 1 - \gamma$ . Item 2 can be shown as follows:

$$p_{b} = \|\Pi |\phi_{b}\rangle |0^{n}\rangle \|^{2}$$

$$= \|\Pi |\phi_{b,0}\rangle |0^{n}\rangle + \Pi |\phi_{b,1}\rangle |0^{n}\rangle \|^{2}$$

$$\leq \|\Pi |\phi_{b,0}\rangle |0^{n}\rangle \|^{2} + \|\Pi |\phi_{b,1}\rangle |0^{n}\rangle \|^{2} + 2\|\Pi |\phi_{b,0}\rangle \| \cdot \|\Pi |\phi_{b,1}\rangle \|$$

$$\leq \|\Pi |\phi_{b,1}\rangle |0^{n}\rangle \|^{2} + 3\gamma^{1/2}$$

where the last inequality follows from  $\|\Pi |\phi_{b,0}\rangle |0^n\rangle \|^2 \leq \|\Pi |\phi_{b,0}\rangle |0^n\rangle \| \leq \gamma^{1/2}$  by Item 1 and  $\|\Pi |\phi_{b,1}\rangle \| \leq 1$ .

We give a lower bound for  $|\langle \psi_0 | \psi_1 \rangle|$ . By the definition of  $|\psi_b\rangle$ ,

$$\begin{split} |\left<\psi_{0}|\psi_{1}\right>| &= |\sqrt{(1-p_{0})(1-p_{1})} + \left<\phi_{0}|\left<0^{n}|\, \varPi\left|\phi_{1}\right>|0^{n}\right>| \\ &= \begin{vmatrix} \sqrt{(1-p_{0})(1-p_{1})} + \left<\phi_{0,0}|\left<0^{n}|\, \varPi\left|\phi_{1,0}\right>|0^{n}\right> + \left<\phi_{0,0}|\left<0^{n}|\, \varPi\left|\phi_{1,1}\right>|0^{n}\right>| \\ &+ \left<\phi_{0,1}|\left<0^{n}|\, \varPi\left|\phi_{1,0}\right>|0^{n}\right> + \left<\phi_{0,1}|\left<0^{n}|\, \varPi\left|\phi_{1,1}\right>|0^{n}\right>| \\ &= \begin{vmatrix} \sqrt{(1-p_{0})(1-p_{1})} + \left<\phi_{0,0}|\left<0^{n}|\, \varPi\left|\phi_{1,0}\right>|0^{n}\right> + \left<\phi_{0,1}|\left<0^{n}|\, \varPi\left|\phi_{1,1}\right>|0^{n}\right>| \\ &+ \left<\phi_{0,1}|\left<0^{n}|\, \left|\phi_{0,1}\right>\right>|0^{n}\right> \\ &+ \left<\phi_{0,1}|\left<0^{n}|\, \varPi\left|\phi_{0,1}\right>|0^{n}\right> + \left<\phi_{0,1}|\left<0^{n}|\, \varPi\left|\phi_{0,1}\right>|0^{n}\right>| \\ &+ \left<\phi_{0,1}|\left<0^{n}|\, \varPi\left|\phi_{1,1}\right> - \left|\phi_{0,1}\right>\right>|0^{n}\right> \\ &+ \left<\phi_{0,1}|\left<0^{n}|\, \varPi\left|\phi_{0,1}\right>|0^{n}\right> + \left<\phi_{0,1}|\left<0^{n}|\, \varPi\left|\phi_{0,1}\right>|0^{n}\right>| \\ &+ \left<\phi_{0,1}|\left<0^{n}|\, \varPi\left|\phi_{0,1}\right>|0^{n}\right>| \\ &+ \left<\phi_{0,1}|\left<0^{n}|\, \varPi\left|\phi_{0,1}\right>|0^{n}\right>| \\ &+ \left<\phi_{0,1}|\left<0^{n}|\, \varPi\left|\phi_{0,1}\right>|0^{n}\right>| \\ &+ \left<\phi_{0,1}|\left<0^{n}|\, \varPi\left|\phi_{0,1}\right>|0^{n}\right>| \\ &+ \left<\phi_{0,1}|\left<0^{n}|\, \varPi\left|\phi_{0,1}\right>|0^$$

{65}------------------------------------------------

where we used the assumption that p<sup>0</sup> ≥ p<sup>1</sup> in the first inequality and [Claim 15](#page-64-1) and the assumption that k |φ1,1i − |φ0,1i k ≤ δ in the second inequality. We note that 1 − 6γ <sup>1</sup>/<sup>2</sup> − δ > 0 since we assume (12γ <sup>1</sup>/<sup>2</sup> + 2δ) <sup>1</sup>/<sup>2</sup> ≤ 1.

Then, we have

$$\| |\psi_0\rangle \langle \psi_0| - |\psi_1\rangle \langle \psi_1| \|_{tr} = \sqrt{1 - |\langle \psi_0|\psi_1\rangle|^2}$$

$$\leq \sqrt{12\gamma^{1/2} + 2\delta}$$

This completes the proof of [Lem. 8.](#page-24-2)
